- **From:** richard@ppeace.demon.co.uk (Richard Tibbetts)
- **Date:** 1998-11-17T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<36522f54.123010792@gateway>`

```
Anyone know for what environment and when the first COBOL (and, for
that matter, other major languages) was available?
```

#### ↳ Re: First COBOL compiler

- **From:** "Warren G. Simmons" <warrens@inficad.com>

```
Richard, a joint demo with Univac, and RCA took place in Philadelphia
and Cherry Hill in 1960-61 time frame. Several programs from a very
few sources were  compiled and run by both companies to demonstrate
to the CODASYL Executive Committee that the compilers and the concept
worked.  I have pictures of the people who were watching the demo, and
a Univac I tape containing a copy of that first compiler that was given
to my boss who was a member of that Exec. Committee. Grace had the
LETTERS COBOL in gold painted on the demo tapes, and RCA created binders
with all of the source code used in the demos.  I used to have
those, but they are probably gone now. However, someone may still have
copies.

Warren Simmons


Richard Tibbetts wrote:

```
About 1966 or 1967 I had a COBOL compiler on a 16K IBM 1401.  At best it
was interesting.  I never did anything useful with it.  By then the
software was already a few years old so I know full well that probably
wasn't close to the earliest implementation, which would have been on
larger machines anyway.
```

were considered mainframes and Cobol was considered a
production language.

- **From:** rkrayhawk@aol.com (RKRayhawk)
- **Date:** 1998-11-17T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<19981117135104.05873.00003253@ng134.aol.com>`
- **References:** `<3652a1e4.18029614@gateway>`

```
The answer is, ...

1) Find the U.S. Navy person who is generally credited with the lead role in
COBOL's evolution. 

2) Hint, it is possible that entering 'Navy' with a search engine harnessed to
comp.lang.cobol, and/or comp.compilers will fish out what you want. That is,
COBOL is not new, and your question is not either.

3) Map out the dates in her life. You then have the COBOL event dates sketched.

4) figure out why the Navy wanted anything to do with COBOL.

5) You can also back track from SNOBOL, and if I'm not mistaken, ALGOL, to
corral dates about COBOL 

6) So, ... although, C does not derrive from COBOL C++ does, because C++
derrives conceptually from Simula, which derives from ALGOL which owes
something to COBOL.
As a consequence another approach might be to try to back track from C++.! ;-) 

7) you could, of course, just try to find out which standards organizations
work with COBOL, and see if their sites have history entries.

8) Please be prepared for some bad news. There is a possibility that when you
get back to the first COBOL compiler that your term compiler becomes
historically a non sequitur.
For 'tis true that the early COBOL work actually influenced the evolution of
the concept of a compiler. One of the most important system concepts that came
from the Navy work was the development (in part) of the idea of addresses that
could be resolved later, stored at addresses that we could know now. 

Such work, to have been done with source expressed  in COBOL syntax, must have
been done before the compiler as an architectural feature of the machine
environment. The early COBOL workers were not alone in their work on the linker
concepts, but they did pioneering work that made compilers as we know them
possible. 

Thus the earliest COBOL syntax processors were not compilers. Intermediate
versions had some compiler features, and not others.

You are probalby after a simple answer like a date.  If you look at the real
pioneering work, you will not have a single date.  They. no doubt. back tracked
in their experiments, and you get into controversy as to what exact combination
of features constitutes a transition from interpreter to compiler.

Perhaps you really just want to know the date of the first commercial compiler
offering. How boring. But that has probably been posted too.

Try using a search engine angainst the relevant newsgroups. And I think the
FAQs for this newsgroups will give you leads to the standards organizations,
which may have pages on history.

9) For extra credit, when and where and who wrote the first linker? What are
the minimum necessary features of a linker? Can a COBOL compiler exist without
a linker?

10) for super-duper credit off the scale: if a debugger will accept commands in
source code syntax, jam them into your executable code and actually execute
them; well then, is that debugger an interpreter, or a compiler and where is
the linker?

Best of luch with your homesork,


Robert Rayhawk
RKRayhawk@aol.com
```

Rick Smith wrote in message ...
>
>Dennis J. Minette wrote in message ...
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Thanks for the memory, Rick.  I'm pretty sure I was using STRING/UNSTRING on
a Honeywell machine circa 1971 in a set of COBOL programs I wrote to parse
COBOL source code and generate sorted listings of all datanames with
references to the program and line number(s) where they were used as well as
the context, or  VERB, with which they were associated.  This capability was
needed to investigate, evaluate and maintain a poorly documented brokerage
"service bureau" package (consisting of a few hundred COBOL programs) that
had just been purchased by my boss at Honeywell Information Services
Division.  At this point in time, I can't think of any other way I could
have easily done it (and as I recall, it was actually rather easily done.)
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: First COBOL compiler

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** iscdavid@aol.com (ISCDavid)

```
Having "been there/done that" I can shed a few bits of light on the questions
asked.

The first COBOL compiler (yes it WAS a true compiler) I saw ran on an IBM 705.
This was in early 1961. The classes were partly taught by "Amazing Grace"
herself.

The Navy...and the Army and the Air force... wanted COBOL because in truth they
are huge businesses with all the problems of any other business, inventory and
payroll being the most pressing at the time.

And finally, up until the IBM 360 I never saw a linker. The compiler just
generated the whole fixed location loadable object.

David
```

Scott Ramey wrote:
> 
> About 1966 or 1967 I had a COBOL compiler on a 16K IBM 1401.  At best it
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> larger machines anyway.

Welcome to the club! My COBOL training was on a 1401 in the spring of
'66 (at IBM's Boston ed center).

I suspect the 1400 implementation was one of IBM's first - the 1400
series dated back to the 50's ('57 or '58ish?) and was by far IBM's most
common system in the field. I worked for IBM as a Systems Engineer from
1965 thru 1969 and can't recall any customers in my branch office who
used COBOL prior to a S/360. In my experience, Autocoder was the
language of choice on the 1400 & 7000 series machines.

Bill Lynch
```

William Lynch wrote:

>I suspect the 1400 implementation was one of IBM's first - the 1400
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>language of choice on the 1400 & 7000 series machines.

In the USAF we didn't even have Autocoder, but had to use SPS.  Didn't
even have multiply/divide hardware, tapes or disk; cards only.  I've
set my share of wordmarks. :)  You could actually see all the discrete
three-legged transistor cases in the 1401 CPU.  Remember those programs
that played music on the 1403 printer? :)  Perhaps it is my age rather
than the times, but it seemed more fun in those days.
```

History of Computer Science
============================

1893:  Ada Lovelace discovers Charles Babbage

1895:  Ada Lovelace discovers the calculator is more fun than Charles Babbage

1950:  COBOL is born; world celebrates

1953:  FORTRAN is born; revenge on COBOL

1977:  C is born; revenge on FORTRAN

1979:  Ada is born; revenge on Charles Babbage

1982:  Death of FORTRAN predicted

1985:  Death of Charles Babbage predicted

1986:  Death of COBOL predicted

1993:  Death of BASIC predicted

#### ↳ Re: First COBOL compiler

- **From:** Greg Hayes <hayford@hayford.demon.co.uk>
- **Date:** 1998-11-18T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bHqGvGA31xU2Ewyg@hayford.demon.co.uk>`
- **References:** `<36522f54.123010792@gateway>`

```
In article <36522f54.123010792@gateway>, Richard Tibbetts
<richard@ppeace.demon.co.uk> writes
>Anyone know for what environment and when the first COBOL (and, for
>that matter, other major languages) was available?


The first COBOL definition was CODASYL 1965.
This rapidly became USASI 1965 (United States of America Standards
Institute)
USASI became ANSI (same thig really)
This was followed by ANSI 1967 (American National Standards Institute)

I wrote 1/4 of COBOL Compiler in 1967 for ICL 4100 (also known as NCR
4100 and Elliot 4100, 4120 & 4130 models).

We used the CODASYL definition. The spec. for COBOL comprises a number
of parts as follows:- (I can't remeber all of them now)

neucleus
environment
table handling
file processing
procedure
etc.

Each one of these had several levels so that the implementor could
choose a level of compliance.

You might choose table handling level 1 which might only allow 2
dimensional occurs or you might choose a higher level giving occurs
depending on, search and search all. File handling levels would cover
peripherals such as paper tape, punch cards, disk drives (although these
were very rare then), mag tapes, printers etc. Our COBOL compiler took a
year approx. and about 10 man years cost. It was contracted out to a
software houe called Jack Harwell Data Processing. Jack Harwell had a
language name after him that he invented while at NCR called Language H.
We were paid 20,000 pounds as a fixed price. We used a large number of
COBOL programs to test the compiler which had been written to test the
RCA spector 70 which became the ICL System 4 (all copies of IBM System
360). We used to write 49 level data structures and write move
statements with 49 A IN B IN C IN D etc. and subscript them 49 times
with each subscript defines as SUB1 IN X IN Y IN Z ..... Then to test
even more we would write the statments as MOVE CORRESPINDING and check
all the generated MOVEs.

It would be interesting to know if many people know how the PERFORM verb
is implemented these days on say IBM mainframes.  I htink that many
people would be surprised at the gnerated code.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: First COBOL compiler

- **From:** Greg Hayes <hayford@hayford.demon.co.uk>
- **Date:** 1998-11-19T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk>`
- **References:** `<36522f54.123010792@gateway> <bHqGvGA31xU2Ewyg@hayford.demon.co.uk> <36535aaa.0@news3.uswest.net>`

```
In article <36535aaa.0@news3.uswest.net>, Chris Osborne
<chris_n_osborne@yahoo.com> writes
>I would be interested in knowing how the PEFORM verb is implemented on
>OS/390 systems.
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>


The COBOL PERFORM verb works as a switch simulating the use of the ALTER
verb.


imagine the following :-

PERFORM AA.


AA.
   lines of coding
   .......
   .......
   etc.

This is normally implemented in the generated code in COBOL terms as:-

   ALTER B TO PROCEED TO AA1.           Set the return address.
   GO TO AA.                            go to the PERFORMed coding
AA1.                                    return label
   ALTER B TO PROCEED TO B1.            reset the drop through address


AA.                                     beginning of PEFORMed coding
lines of coding
   .......
   .......
   etc.


B.                                      label for return coding
   GO TO B1.                            return/drop through code
B1.                                     drop through label



This flip flop switch mechanism means that the PERFORM activates the
return mechanism and after it has returned it deactivates it. This then
means that if the coding that was performed is reached in a sequential
manner by dropping through it rather than performing it then the coding
will continue onwards into the next statements.  This method is almost
universal now.


A small aside.  For all those people who use lots of flags in their
programs and say that they cannot do without them, I have seen a proof
that all coding can be rearranged to eliminate all flags except one (the
first time through flag).
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: First COBOL compiler

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** rkrayhawk@aol.com (RKRayhawk)
- **Date:** 1998-11-20T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<19981120173654.17481.00000722@ng135.aol.com>`
- **References:** `<3654ae62.0@news3.uswest.net>`

```
Okay. What is wrong with this picture painted with these words: 
"...we need EXIT paragraph labels so that we can have something for out GOTOs
to go to... "? 
No, that is not for extra credit, you get a demerit if you as much as even
undertake to consider it. Bad programmer! Bad!

And bandwidth, what about bandwidth? One line for the space between the real
paragraph and the exit paragraph label, (1). One line for the EXIT statement,
(2). Maybe one line for those geniuses that put a period on a separate line for
'easy maintainability', (maybe 3, and surely there could not be a more inane
application of such vacuity). And last but not least, coders, we need another
blank line after the exit paragraph, for 'readability', (3, maybe 4 lines).

((and incidentally there is actually an implicit wasted line supra at the
nefarious GOTO which is our justification in some cases))

For human-machine interaction there are three basic dimmensions of the visual
interface, vertical, horizontal and depth (which we achieve with the
magnificent smart editors that allow context sensitive coloration).

PC editors are highly flexilble and can be configured to display numerous lines
per page/window.  But there are human limits, especially for old geezers.  Four
lines is 10% of a forty line screen (approx 8% of a 50 line screen).

Many people use displays with about thirty lines per screen.  And there are a
few threads contemporaneous with this in this newsgroup wherein a few workers
imply that they are actually using 25 line display modes on the mainframe (as
though mod 4 and mod 5 where unheard of).

We need to factor into this a very difficult consideration of split screen and
window superposition, robbing a display mode of available vertical realestate.

But no matter how you cut it, 4 lines or so commited to do nothing at all is a
considerable waste of the vertical aspect of the human-machine interface.  And
I am very convinced that this is not a theoretical consideration at all. The
gestalt of a screen that can present a whole algorithm at once on the screen in
contrast to fractions, is much more productive.   

It is like reading. If you read letters, you almost can't get the point. If you
read words or phrases, the speed of the flow changes the sense really.

It is symptomatic that proponents of EXIT paragraphs are pre-occupied with
miniscule mechanics like GOTOs. They are frequently missing the flow at the
PERFORM level. They are not thinking in the most productive terms, IMHO. 

This is a cost factor.  Not just measurable in the keystrokes to generate
functionless code, but in the rate of comprehension upon code review either in
final program generation or maintenance.

To a lesser extent, the presence of the THRU clause is a horizontal dimmension
inefficiency, that causes an objective and measurable dimimution of code
comprehension.

((And just for chuckles, it is worth noting that the verbosity of some primary
paragraph names, combining with indentation, makes it sometimes necessary to
place the THRU clause on a separate line the PERFORM verb itself; again
draining vertical realestate)).

I am sure that it is possible to 'see' code at a higher level than statements -
infact indentation is a means to promote that. Collections of statements have
graphical aspects. Wasted vertical space definitely damages the potential to
perceive the surface of a paragraph.

Such perception is enhanced by narrow lines, and threatened by split screens,
window superpositions, irrepressible tool icon 'lines', consumptive window
frames (and even hot spots on modern ISPF screens which take up lines, which
are next to impossible to get rid of because ISPF now executes in an 'improved'
mark-up language context).

I know that it seems theoretical to some folks. I respect that. And it is also
easy to agree with the several comments to the effect that sticking to a
consistent style is an aid to productivity for the code originator and
maintainer.

But Greg Hayes' nutshell explanation of the pervasive compiler technique
deployed to implement PERFORM, does us the service of removing the _foundation_
of this discussion from the realm of subjectivity.

It is a fact that the compiler is already, always!, doing THRU logic. It is
already there! Coding THRU is redundant. This is simply objective.

I would not advocate that people who use THRU/EXIT actually change, I know that
is hard for you. I am just encouraging you to realize the import of Mr. Hayes'
excellent, and emotion free explanation.

You do not have to agree with anyone's implied proposal to get rid of this
stuff.  Not at all, don't agree to anything. But if you choose to, you can look
at the fact that the THRU clause has no purpose and the lines used to code the
EXIT paragraph, are using space that can be used to display meaningful code.

I would not ask for even the slightest compromise here.  For example, if you
are one of those who like to put periods on separate lines, and you like
THRU/EXIT coding style.
Then, for you, you should definitely code that period after the EXIT statement
on a sepatrate line.

And there is a special variation on this that is true museumware. Some folks
like to separate the period from the rest of the last statement at the end of a
paragraph, so "we can see it."

Res ipsa loquitor,

Robert Rayhawk
RKRayhawk@aol.com
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EXIT PARAGRAPH (was: First COBOL compiler)

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Leif Svalgaard" <leif@ibm.net>
- **Date:** 1998-11-20T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3655f81c.0@news1.ibm.net>`
- **References:** `<3654ae62.0@news3.uswest.net> <19981120173654.17481.00000722@ng135.aol.com>`

```
Since this side-thread already has strayed far from the original
subject I feel no guilt in 'hi-jacking' it with a new subject (trying
to join it with another thread on the eternal subject of EXIT and
GO TOs).

RKRayhawk wrote in message <19981120173654.17481.00000722@ng135.aol.com>...
>Okay. What is wrong with this picture painted with these words:
>"...we need EXIT paragraph labels so that we can have something for out
GOTOs
>to go to... "?
, if you>No, that is not for extra credit, you get a demerit if you as much
as even
>undertake to consider it. Bad programmer! Bad!
>I would not ask for even the slightest compromise here>
>And there is a special variation on this that is true museumware. Some
folks
>like to separate the period from the rest of the last statement at the end
of a
>paragraph, so "we can see it."

To use a word that has been use a lot in the recent impeachment crisis,
the 'framers' of Cobol felt that it should read like text and provided for
the
use of commas (,), semicolons (;) and periods (dots, full-stops, etc) (.)
as an aid to enhance the understanding of the text, just as these
punctuation marks help make ordinary text (like this one) more readable.
Commas and semicolons have always been 'white space' with no syntactical
role; unfortunately, periods were used to end IF clauses and were (are)
mandatory at the end of a paragraph. The latter is completely superfluous,
as the next paragraph (or end of source) serves to do that. That leaves
the IF clause. With the END-IF delimiter, the period is also superfluous
for IF-clauses.

That leaves the period at the end of the paragraph as the only one
you need to have. It is the (not needed) exit point, if you like, of the
paragraph
and does not belong to and therefore was never separated from the last
statement of the paragraphs.
One of the most common errors is a missing, misplaced, or extra period.
Get rid of all of them (except the exit point as it unfortunately is still
required).
```

The whole world should be grateful for the post of Greg Hayes
<hayford@hayford.demon.co.uk>
On Thu, 19 Nov 1998 11:09:51 +0000


>>

The COBOL PERFORM verb works as a switch simulating the use of the ALTER
verb.


imagine the following :-

PERFORM AA.


AA.
   lines of coding
   .......
   .......
   etc.

   GO TO AA.                            go to the PERFORMed coding

<<


If you study the concept that Greg Hayes has explained you will realize that
100% of all THRU clauses in statement like

  PERFORM 100-PARA-A THRU 100-EXIT.

(where paragraph 100-PARA-A is a simple single paragraph followed immediately
by paragraph  100-EXIT which is as follows!)

100-EXIT.
      EXIT.

are ludicrous absurdities as are the 100-EXIT paragraph label and the EXIT
statement.  There are billions of these things out there!

And even Carl Sagan could not have possibly rendered that plosive 'b'
sufficiently emphatic.

Surely THRU has its legitimate uses, but EXIT is useless; or worse, really
misleading to those who have not or cannot learned this lesson.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: First COBOL compiler

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** Greg Hayes <hayford@hayford.demon.co.uk>
- **Date:** 1998-11-19T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<OA+zuDAghGV2EwI8@hayford.demon.co.uk>`
- **References:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk> <19981119102634.09602.00000036@ng-cr1.aol.com>`

```
In article <19981119102634.09602.00000036@ng-cr1.aol.com>, RKRayhawk
<rkrayhawk@aol.com> writes
>
>The whole world should be grateful for the post of Greg Hayes
…[76 more quoted lines elided]…
>

The safest way to produce clean code is to restrict PERFORMs to
performing whole SECTIONS. From the SECTION then PERFORM other SECTIONs.
This used to be called top down modularity. The EXIT is then only used
for premature exit of a SECTION. The original intention was for this to
be used as a destination for a GO TO.




By the way did anyone realize that when COBOL was defined all verbs were
unique in their first 2 characters to make syntax checking easier. This
rue was broken in a later definition by IBM who introduced an extension.
I cannot think at the moment which verb it was. I know that the addition 
of the ON verb did cause some problems, as in :-

ON 1 AND EVERY 3 imperative statment.

This was a debugging statment.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: First COBOL compiler

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** Howard Brazee <NOSPAMbrazee@home.com>
- **Date:** 1998-11-19T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<365477EF.9A08F9F0@home.com>`
- **References:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk> <19981119102634.09602.00000036@ng-cr1.aol.com> <OA+zuDAghGV2EwI8@hayford.demon.co.uk>`

```
> The safest way to produce clean code is to restrict PERFORMs to
> performing whole SECTIONS. From the SECTION then PERFORM other SECTIONs.
> This used to be called top down modularity. The EXIT is then only used
> for premature exit of a SECTION. The original intention was for this to
> be used as a destination for a GO TO.

I disagree.  The safest way to produce clean code is to eliminate
sections and only perform individual paragraphs.  Never perform THRU, no
GO TO exit.  With sorts, I have the required sections followed by a
shell section containing all normal paragraphs.  Never, never drop into
a paragraph.  The first paragraph of the program exits the program.

Maintenance programmers will put new paragraphs where they can be
dropped into accidently if you perform paragraph groups.

> By the way did anyone realize that when COBOL was defined all verbs were
> unique in their first 2 characters to make syntax checking easier. This
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> This was a debugging statment.

I miss that statement.  But programming IDMS, I also get lots of "ON
DB-ANY-ERROR", so I can't exclude all lines and look for " on " to
quickly locate where I put the statement without getting a bunch of
extra lines.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: First COBOL compiler

_(reply depth: 7)_

- **From:** Howard Brazee <NOSPAMbrazee@home.com>
- **Date:** 1998-11-20T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: First COBOL compiler

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** iscdavid@aol.com (ISCDavid)
- **Date:** 1998-11-20T00:00:00

- **Message-ID:** `<19981120102149.02128.00000216@ng-fu1.aol.com>`
- **References:** `<OA+zuDAghGV2EwI8@hayford.demon.co.uk>`

```
I don't know what IBM generates now for PERFORM, but the original 360
architecture stored the return address in a register (can't for the life of me
remember the actual op code now). The first thing the performed routine did was
to save the register And I think increment it to get to the next operand, then
the exit from the perform was to reload the register and do a Branch Register
to return.

See why GO TOs could be VERY dangerous in those days??:)

BTW...way back then I could never understand how COBOL programmers who never
coded a 360 in BAL cound debug anything. Some of the first 360 COBOL compilers
could and did screw up when generating machine code...like store the operands
backward in a cascading op.

David Olson
```

That would be a strange compiler warning considering that EXIT is supposed
to be the _only_ COBOL element within the paragraph in which it is placed.
It is essentially a NOP that allows use of the paragraph that holds it to
serve as the destination of a GO TO or as the yyyy paragraph a PERFORM xxxx
THRU yyyy statement.

Dennis J. Minette wrote:
> 
> Frank Swarbrick wrote in message <36550897.E69@sprynet.com>...
…[36 more quoted lines elided]…
> THRU yyyy statement.

I'm not sure what you mean.  With the IBM VS COBOL II compiler, if I compile
a program that looks like the following:

PROCEDURE DIVISION.
    PERFORM 1000-MAIN THRU 1000-EXIT.
    PERFORM 2000-CLEANUP THRU 2000-EXIT.
    STOP RUN.

1000-MAIN.
    DISPLAY 'THIS IS A TEST'.

1000-EXIT.

2000-CLEANUP.
    DISPLAY 'AND SO IS THIS.

2000-EXIT.

I will get a warning that the paragraph before 2000-CLEANUP goes not contain
any statements.  (Not sure if I'd get one for the second exit paragraph
since there are no paragraphs following it.)  If I add an EXIT statement
after each EXIT paragraph header I will not get the warning(s).

I do realize that EXIT doesn't actually exit the paragraph necessarily
(which is about the craziest thing I've ever heard, especially concidering 
that COBOL II does not force you to make it the only statement in a paragraph).

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EXIT PARAGRAPH, PERFORM...THRU etc. (was: First COBOL compiler)

_(reply depth: 7)_

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc123@bellsouth.net>
- **Date:** 1998-11-20T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<lSl52.587$Gy.710084@news4.mia>`
- **References:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk> <19981119102634.09602.00000036@ng-cr1.aol.com> <Tpc52.297$Sf1.435979@news3.mia> <3655866a.0@news3.uswest.net>`

```
Chris Osborne wrote:
>
>Judson McClendon wrote in message ...
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>What are those theoretical and practical reasons?

We had a long thread on this a while back, and I don't want to get it
flamed up again, but the primary theoretical reason is that I subscribe
to Yourdon's definition of good structured code: all code in code blocks
with one entry point and one exit point.  As you know, most structured languages like Pascal, C, etc. have some sort of 'exit block'
statement,
which is lacking in the current COBOL standard.  I use GO TO xxx-EXIT
as the 'exit block' construct.  I agree that with no other checks, it
opens up the possibility of a GO TO outside the paragraph by accident.
To prevent this I use a simple code scanner which checks for a GO TO
to the wrong paragraph, and for PERFORM ... THRU ... where the THRU
paragraph is not the correct EXIT paragraph.  The scanner also checks
for missing or extra periods.  Some of my use of this would be reduced,
though not eliminated, if my clients all used COBOL 85 with END-xxx,
but my two major clients are still using COBOL 74, which is one of the
practical reasons.  Not only myself, but my clients' programmers and a
number of other shops in this area use this technique.  Several of the
shops simply started using it because they had heard how well it
worked with my clients.  The other primary practical reason is that I
have been watching shops adopt this technique for some 20 years, and
it *works*.  Using my programming methods as a whole, not just the
PERFORM/THRU, in every case programmer productivity increased noticeably
and error levels went way down.  Which is why the techniques have
spread by word of mouth, even to shops where I never consulted.  The
final main practical reason for using it is that I have tried avoiding
the use of GO TO EXIT, and you simply get more code, overall.  It may
depend a lot on what type program you are doing, but in the programs
I frequently write, there is a great deal of code where you have a
long paragraph if field error tests, each with an exit if an error is
found.  Coding this in standard COBOL without the GO TO EXIT simply
takes more code.  As soon as we get an EXIT PARAGRAPH construct in the
standard, it will eliminate the need for this, and I will drop it. :-)

> Strictly from my point of view, they take up screen space when I am looking
>at code, and since I can code without them, not typing in "100-EXIT. EXIT."
>or "THRU" (or even copying them and pasting the EXIT lines) saves me time.

Well, so does the code you write to avoid it. :-)  You just don't think
of it as 'extra' code.

>I would like to know in what way you use them to enhance your coding speed,
>code reliability, etc.

I partially answered that above.  One way I use this to increase my
productivity is that much of the online programming I do has a lot
of field validation code which I can do using data dictionary code
I developed a few years ago.  This means that validating most fields
is as simple as move the field name and contents into specified data
fields, perform the dictionary edit routine and GO TO EXIT on error.
I can extract the field names from the database definition to create
a little file of the names.  I then run a BASIC program which
generates the code for each field.  Not only does this enormously
increase my productivity for that part of the coding, it is virtually
error-free.  I average around 200,000 lines of COBOL code per year,
only because not everything I do is new development, and I also do a
lot of systems design.  If I spent my time solely at new development
coding, I could probably turn out around 1,000,000 lines of code per
year, using these methods.  I have to admit that I work hard on my
productivity, and consistency, consistency, consistency.  Every time
you avoid having to make a decision, you avoid a potential error and
you do it faster.  Every time you can utilize a habit positively, you
do it faster and with less errors.  Making the program listing as
clear and readable as possible to the eye means speed and less errors.
Visually efficient does not necessarily mean pretty, but it often is.
It means using the way the brain and eye function to arrange your code
so it is quickly and easily visually recognized and assimilated.  Even
though personal tastes differ greatly, it is surprising how consistent
some practices can be in improving readability.  Form follows function,
always.  Don't go for what pleases the eye, go for what the eye sees
clearer and easier; not the same thing.

I know this is anecdotal, but last year and half of this year an old
friend helped me write a $400,000 system.  He is a computer scientist,
MS in CS, has taught CS at the university level, 20 years in and around
NASA, mostly FORTRAN, C, etc.  When I started teaching him to use my
programming standards, he kicked and screamed, and I thought he was
never going to accept it.  I finally got him to just go along until the
system was done.  When we first started bringing up the code for testing,
he was constantly remarking on how few bugs there were.  Mind you, this
was a *difficult* system!  I have done larger systems, but never one
more difficult, and my friend told me the only system he had ever seen
which compared in difficulty was an enormously complex solution of
simultaneous equations for a stress analysis of a huge structure for
NASA.  He said two whole teams of programmers literally quit without
getting it to work.  After 18 months, we brought our system up live,
and there were very few minor bugs.  The client was ecstatic.  Later
I asked my friend what he thought of my programming standards now, and
he said "Man, I hate to think of doing that system without them!"  He's
hooked, what more can I say? :-)

Whew!  Sorry for such a long post.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EXIT PARAGRAPH, PERFORM...THRU etc. (was: First COBOL compiler)

_(reply depth: 9)_

- **From:** redsky@ibm.net (Thane Hubbell)
- **Date:** 1998-11-21T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<365642f4.10363517@news1.ibm.net>`
- **References:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk> <19981119102634.09602.00000036@ng-cr1.aol.com> <Tpc52.297$Sf1.435979@news3.mia> <3655866a.0@news3.uswest.net> <lSl52.587$Gy.710084@news4.mia> <3655EDF2.53A91073@home.com>`

```
On Fri, 20 Nov 1998 15:32:18 -0700, Howard Brazee
<NOSPAMbrazee@home.com> wrote:

>The client was ecstatic.  Later
>> I asked my friend what he thought of my programming standards now, and
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>changed my coding to eliminate GOTO 1111-EXIT statements.

I concur - sort of.  It takes a different method of thinking.  There
are occasionally extra tests.  BUT a different design (not implying
there is anything wrong with the "other" way) is required.  Things
tend to be broken down a little more.  Tests are done eariler in the
process.  Overall I can't say which is more efficient - although I can
say which I prefer.  One thing that is a MUST is consistency.  You
CANNOT mix these two approaches with any success.

Let me try to illustrate.

Recently I had to write a program to access an Oracle database using
SQL.  Knowing no SQL - I could spell it - I did some reading and
looking at sample programs.

The samples that other programmers had put together used Perform ...
Thru.  When errors such as Not found and SQL ERROR are encountered,
the coded the exception handling with GO TO.  

I decided not to use that approach, but instead my chosen style. I
like the result.  It's clean and it is actually LESS lines of code -
but more compares, than the other style.

Why?  

My program is a called program that reads a single record via a key
form the database.  It does a select into.  If the record is found the
data is moved into the linkage section to be passed back to the
calling program.  If not found, a flag is set so indicating - and the
reason - database error or truly not found.

You have to code the exception handler first - I perform a paragraph
when not found, and another for SQL error - setting the switch.

Then the select is coded.

I have to FIRST set the switch indicating the record is FOUND, then do
the select, then compare the switch to decide if I should move the
record to the linkage area or not.  If I had used the GO TO method,
this compare would be unnecessary.

So what I think generally happens is that "extra" comparisons are used
to make up for the GO TO.

Hope this makes sense!
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EXIT PARAGRAPH, PERFORM...THRU etc. (was: First COBOL compiler)

_(reply depth: 10)_

- **From:** William Lynch <wblynch@att.net>
- **Date:** 1998-11-21T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<36565804.5552F328@att.net>`
- **References:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk> <19981119102634.09602.00000036@ng-cr1.aol.com> <Tpc52.297$Sf1.435979@news3.mia> <3655866a.0@news3.uswest.net> <lSl52.587$Gy.710084@news4.mia> <3655EDF2.53A91073@home.com> <365642f4.10363517@news1.ibm.net>`

```
Thane Hubbell wrote:
(snip)
> 
> So what I think generally happens is that "extra" comparisons are used
> to make up for the GO TO.
> 
> Hope this makes sense!

Indeed! In my experience those who complain about about using simple
PERFORMs and no GOTOs are the ones who are doing their decision making
at the bottom of a paragraph instead of in the code which PERFORMs the
paragraph. They tend to end up writing Assembler code in COBOL notation;
adding +1 to a subscript, comparing to the limit (*always* a literal),
and GOTOing the paragraph name to loop. They're trying to control the
process from the bottom instead of from the top. 

Bill Lynch
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EXIT PARAGRAPH, PERFORM...THRU etc. (was: First COBOL compiler)

_(reply depth: 11)_

- **From:** jcj0347@aol.com (JCJ0347)
- **Date:** 1998-11-24T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<19981124012414.01706.00002084@ng121.aol.com>`
- **References:** `<36565804.5552F328@att.net>`

```
>They tend to end up writing Assembler code in COBOL notation;
>adding +1 to a subscript, comparing to the limit (*always* a literal),
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>Bill Lynch

Yep.  I spent a lot of years going around modifying existing code,
and I found a lot of this.  Never bothered me too much because of
all the blankety-blank Assembler I had to take in school.  Easy to
read, a nightmare to maintain or enhance. 

Also saw a lot of PERFORM THRU style coding.  I personally don't
use this style, but once you get inside the head of the person that
wrote it, it's not too bad to maintain.

Generally speaking, poor PD coding will also be accompanied by
sorry data names and ambigious paragraph names.

Good PD coding will also go hand-in-hand with meaningful data
names and carefully thought out prefixes for the fields defined
for the data file records.

JC Jones.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EXIT PARAGRAPH, PERFORM...THRU etc. (was: First COBOL compiler)

_(reply depth: 9)_

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc123@bellsouth.net>
- **Date:** 1998-11-21T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<WHA52.1080$Sf1.1071121@news3.mia>`
- **References:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk> <19981119102634.09602.00000036@ng-cr1.aol.com> <Tpc52.297$Sf1.435979@news3.mia> <3655866a.0@news3.uswest.net> <lSl52.587$Gy.710084@news4.mia> <3655EDF2.53A91073@home.com>`

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
>The client was ecstatic.  Later
>> I asked my friend what he thought of my programming standards now,
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>Did he have one before?

Absolutely.  He taught CS at the university level for several years,
and worked on a number of large and complex projects for NASA.  He is
very well founded in structured theory and design.  At first he did
not see how some of the constraints my standards make were going to
help.  It was only after he begin to see the results that he became
enthusiastic.  When I first started teaching my programming standards
to a number of programmers working for me about 20 years ago, it was
like pulling teeth.  All of them were reluctant, and almost refused
outright to do it.  After several months, you could not have paid any
of them to go back.  Even after 20 years, some of them still mention
this to me with a smile.

There is nothing wrong with speaking from a theoretical standpoint,
but we need to remember it is just theory until it is put to the test
of reality.  That's why I have little patience for those who hack at
these methods without having given them a good trial.  I have seen
too much benefit to too many programmers in too many shops for too
many years (too many toos? :) to be swayed by theoretical arguments
from those who have not tried it.  Theory is tested against reality,
not the other way around. :-)  I don't doubt that there may be other
methods as good, perhaps even better.  But I know without doubt that
these methods work, and work very well.  In practice, I have yet to
see anything else which beats them.  When I do, I'll change.  But I
have to see it actually work first. :-)  I know some of you would
agree with me that our profession has taken up the banner of OO on
mostly theoretical grounds, and the results have simply not born out
the hype.  What a waste.  Make sure that whatever you are changing
to will deliver the goods before you bet the house on it.
```

```
Judson McClendon wrote in message ...

>There is nothing wrong with speaking from a theoretical standpoint,
>but we need to remember it is just theory until it is put to the test
>of reality.  That's why I have little patience for those who hack at
>these methods without having given them a good trial.  I have seen


Actually, I coded with the one entry and one exit paragraph for
about five years, the rule being that go to's were allowed, but
only to the start paragraph or to the end paragraph.  I found it
worked well, but I did not like the looks of it.

Over a period of time, I found that I evolved ways of coding that
bypassed the need for the exit paragraph, and gradually dropped
it.  There is very little point of that exit paragraph if one writes
half a million lines of code, and in the entire lot of it not a single
exit paragraph is actually referenced.  When you get to that
stage, then it is redundant, and detracts.

I think the point is that some kind of discipline is needed to keep
code well structured, and that that discipline needs to be emphatically
enforced.  There are, however, different ways of doing it.

The methodology involved influences that heavily as well.  For
example, event driven stuff tends to force huge loops of
event checkers that may be better suited to one methodology
than another.
```

You have over the (many) years developed a style and discipline (supported

style, so for people just getting into Cobol, I would say that a GO TO
less style with no THRU, no EXIT, no 'fall through', no SECTIONS,
no ALTER (sorry, couldn't resist) would be the better way to go,
notwithstanding the fact that this would cramp *your* style.

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc123@bellsouth.net>
- **Date:** 1998-11-21T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<OmB52.1091$Sf1.1090000@news3.mia>`
- **References:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk> <19981119102634.09602.00000036@ng-cr1.aol.com> <Tpc52.297$Sf1.435979@news3.mia> <3655866a.0@news3.uswest.net> <lSl52.587$Gy.710084@news4.mia> <3655f11c.0@news1.ibm.net>`

```
Leif Svalgaard wrote:
>
>You have over the (many) years developed a style and discipline (supported
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>notwithstanding the fact that this would cramp *your* style.

Understand, I'm not suggesting that everybody start using my style. :-)
I only got involved in this thread in response to a comment that using
PERFORM/THRU and exit paragraphs were "ludicrous absurdities".  To me,
that statement is a "ludicrous absurdity". :-)  But I would counterpoint
your comments in two areas.

First, before I settled on PERFORM/THRU and GO TO xxx-EXIT I was very
enthusiastic about structured coding (still am :), and was bent on
going completely GOTO-less.  In fact, I programmed without GO TO and
PERFORM/THRU for well over a year, perhaps two.  I was already an
accomplished programmer, with about 10 years experience.  Eventually I
realized that I was writing more code, more complex and less clear code,
and using many more paragraph names, than I would if I used the GOTO
exit for error exits.  So I have not been reluctant to accept GOTO-less
programming, I reluctantly retreated from using it in COBOL after one
to two years of serious effort.  I do not use GOTO in other languages
except assembly, and will stop using it and PERFORM/THRU in COBOL when
EXIT PARAGRAPH is available. :-)

Second, it is not just how well it works for me, it is how well it
works for dozens of other programmers in a number of shops who have
adopted these styles, and how well *they* think it works.  There are at
least a half dozen shops in this area that have adopted my programming
standards entirely by word of mouth.  I have visited shops where I had
never been before, seen a program listing coming out on the printer,
thinking "Wow, that looks like my code", to find they had simply adopted
my style.  Would people do that if they had not seen objective results
quite apart from just me?
```

_(reply depth: 11)_

- **From:** "Michael C. Kasten" <mck9@swbell.net>
- **Date:** 1998-11-21T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<36579F79.329A@swbell.net>`
- **References:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk> <19981119102634.09602.00000036@ng-cr1.aol.com> <Tpc52.297$Sf1.435979@news3.mia> <3655866a.0@news3.uswest.net> <lSl52.587$Gy.710084@news4.mia> <3655f11c.0@news1.ibm.net> <OmB52.1091$Sf1.1090000@news3.mia> <3656ed2c.0@news1.ibm.net>`

```
Leif Svalgaard wrote:

[snip; some friendly sparring between Leif and Judson]

> I think that having *a* style is better than having *no* style. Consistency
> in and of itself is good, so no quibble here. I also think that what we are
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> GO TO xxxx-EXIT to show us how useful it is?

Judson posted such an example about a year ago, I think, in a
forerunner of this thread.  His code validated a long series of data
entry fields, with a repetitive and stereotyped pattern of coding.
Whenever it found an invalid field it would issue a message and GO TO 
an EXIT paragraph.

At least two responses (one of them mine) pointed out that he could
rearrange his test a bit with no increase in code, using an IF to
bypass each validation if any previous field had been invalid.  He
didn't like that solution, largely on the grounds that it was less
efficient.  Once you find an error, you have to test the flag umpteen 
times all the way down to the bottom of the paragraph, instead of 
bailing out immediately.

(Somebody posted another solution: adopt a one-period-per-paragraph
style, and use NEXT SENTENCE instead of GO TO XXXX-EXIT.  Personally
I think NEXT SENTENCE is almost as ugly as GO TO, and I would worry
that someone would change it to CONTINUE without recognizing the
consequences.  However, it's an interesting idea, and it responds to
Judson's concern for efficiency.)

I hope I am summarizing his position accurately.  I still wouldn't
code that way myself unless I was desperate to squeeze out every
nanosecond, but it's one of the least offensive uses of GO TO that
I can think of.

There are a couple of other aspects to his style which mitigate the
use of GO TO.  He has mentioned them from time to time, but I don't 
think the stylistic implications have been fully recognized.

First: he uses a code generator, of his own devising, to write much of 
the routine and stereotyped code.  The impact on productivity is
obvious, but there is also an impact on style.  To the extent that
you rely on a generator, the style of the generated code is 
irrelevant.  What matters is the style of whatever you feed into the 
generator, especially if you later maintain the program at that level 
rather than at the level of the generated code.

Second: he uses a code checker, also of his own devising, to enforce
restrictions on the use of GO TO.  One of the main problems with GO TO
is that it is so prone to undisciplined abuse.  If you're studying an
unfamiliar program containing GO TOs, it takes considerable analysis
to determine whether all the GO TOs are used in a consistent and
responsible way.  Even a single errant GO TO or PERFORM THRU can
make the code behave in unexpected ways, due to implicit fall-throughs 
from one paragraph to the next.  However, if the program has passed 
through Judson's code checker, you no longer need to worry about these
pernicious possibilities.

In a sense, then, Judson isn't really coding in COBOL.  Either he's
feeding something into his code generator or he's coding in a 
restricted subset of COBOL.  Within that subset, precisely because
of the restrictions, GO TO is a benign synonym for EXIT PARAGRAPH.

For those of us who code in full unrestricted COBOL (unleashed?),
Judson's stylistic recommendations carry less weight.  We're not
coding in quite the same language.

Michael C. Kasten	mck9@swbell.net
http://home.swbell.net/mck9/cobol/cobol.html
```

Re: efficiency. The following two compiler listings of generated code
(16 bit Realia on PC) speak for themselves:

002300     PERFORM PARA1 THRU PARA1-EXIT
002400     STOP RUN
002500     .
002600
002700 PARA1.
002800     IF MY-VAR = "X"
002900         GO TO PARA1-EXIT
003000     ELSE
003100     IF MY-VAR = "Y"
003200         MOVE "Z" TO MY-VAR
003300     .
003400 PARA1-EXIT.
003500     EXIT
003600     .
                                ;   23 PERFORM
 0048  B8 0050                            MOV    AX, OFFSET L8
 004B  A3 00C8                            MOV    W+200, AX
 004E  EB 05                              JMP    SHORT L4
                                ;   24 STOP
 0050  9A 0000 ---- E           L8:       CALL   RCL$SR
                                ;   28 IF
 0055  A0 00C6                  L4:       MOV    AL, B+198
 0058  3C 58                              CMP    AL, 58H
                                ;   29 GO TO
 005A  74 0C                              JE     L5
                                ;   30 ELSE (move snipped)
...                             ;   35 EXIT
 0068  B8 0071                  L5:       MOV    AX, OFFSET L6
 006B  87 06 00C8                         XCHG   AX, W+200
 006F  FF E0                              JMP    AX
 0071  A1 0000                  L6:

002300     PERFORM PARA1
002400     STOP RUN
002500     .
002600
002700 PARA1.
002800     IF MY-VAR = "X"
002900         NEXT SENTENCE
003000     ELSE
003100     IF MY-VAR = "Y"
003200         MOVE "Z" TO MY-VAR
003300     .
                                ;   23 PERFORM
 0048  E8 0050 R                          CALL   L4
                                ;   24 STOP
 004B  9A 0000 ---- E                     CALL   RCL$SR
                                ;   28 IF
 0050  A0 00C6                  L4:       MOV    AL, B+198
 0053  3C 58                              CMP    AL, 58H
                                ;   29 NEXT
 0055  74 0C                              JE     L8
                                ;   30 ELSE (move snipped)
 0063  C3                       L8:       RET

---------------------------------------------
>
>There are a couple of other aspects to his style which mitigate the
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>coding in quite the same language.


I think you have a good point here.
There is, of course, nothing wrong with GO TOs in generated code
(the compiler itself issues jump instructions), as long as the generated
code is not maintained directly. If what you maintain is the input to
your generator, everything is fine and style is completely moot as the
generated code is not meant for human consumption.

If, on the other hand, you use your code generator to generate source
and THEN turn that source over to your client to be maintained by him,
then style becomes important again and all the above applies.

Using the generator to generate code that is LESS efficient than the
GO TO free code does not seem to be a desirable way to go. IMHO.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EXIT PARAGRAPH, PERFORM...THRU etc. (was: First COBOL compiler)

_(reply depth: 13)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 1998-11-22T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<73a5f6$643@dfw-ixnews6.ix.netcom.com>`
- **References:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk> <19981119102634.09602.00000036@ng-cr1.aol.com> <Tpc52.297$Sf1.435979@news3.mia> <3655866a.0@news3.uswest.net> <lSl52.587$Gy.710084@news4.mia> <3655f11c.0@news1.ibm.net> <OmB52.1091$Sf1.1090000@news3.mia> <3656ed2c.0@news1.ibm.net> <36579F79.329A@swbell.net> <36586f1b.0@news1.ibm.net>`

```
I just thought that I would jump in here with a comment on this (and the
almost monthly similar) threads.  Some things they point out to me are:

A) The EXIT PARAGRAPH/SECTION syntax being introduced into the next Standard
really will meet an outstanding need.  There has been talk about some of the
problems (and they do exist) that this new syntax will allow and whether or
not they should be included.  These threads convince me that they really are
a good feature.

B) Programmers can write good code and programmers can write bad code.  The
available styles and even the available syntax simply do NOT prevent bad
programmers from writing bad code - nor does the lack of a feature stop good
programmers from writing good code.

C) "Style" issues (like what editor to use) really are a "religious" issue.
Even those who have used many different styles, have their favorites and -
although they may "preach" that you use the right style for the right
problem - most of us really are more comfortable with one style (or editor
or type of syntax or compiler or ...) and will defend it (and propagate it)
whenever and wherever we can.

****

The following is my summary of "styles" that if used consistently work (and
which avoid error-prone coding - as long as programs using them are
maintained in the same style in which they were written):

1) Always use PERFORM Para thru Para-Exit coding.  (No sections ever used).
The only Go To that is allowed is a Go To Para-Exit.  No other paragraph
name is allowed between Para and Para-Exit.

2) Never code the "thru" phrase of Perform.  Only Perform paragraphs (no
sections allowed).  Within any paragraph if a condition occurs that causes
you to want to avoid any additional logic, then
   A) use a flag and all subsequent tests in that paragraph start out with
that test (or if you are using all the '85 Standard positive and negative
conditionals and END-scope terminators - then just "nest" the rest of your
paragraph's logic).
   B) use the new EXIT PARAGRAPH (currently an extension in some compilers -
coming generally in the next Standard) to exit immediately
  C) Use "next Sentence" to get to the end of the paragraph that has only
one period in it.
  D) Nest levels of paragraphs, so each time a "I want to get out of this
paragraph" situation arises, the rest of that "routines" logic is placed in
another paragraph that is performed while for the exception case, logic
falls thru to the end of the original paragraph.

3) Only perform Sections - never allow the THRU phrase.  Within each Section
you have one paragraph and its exit.  Then if an "exception" (get me out
quick) situation arises, you GO TO the exit paragraph.

****

Any one of these styles (or sub-styles) should work if used consistently.
Some of these MAY produce more efficient code under some compilers - but the
efficiency is likely to change from compiler to compiler.  Any one of these
can (and will) be error-prone if someone comes along to maintain the program
and is used to (and tries to use) one of the other styles.

OK - folks,
  Have I missed any of the alternatives?  Does anyone really think that
someone from one camp is going to EVER convince people in another camp?
Anyone want to guess how soon after this thread dies down, the same
"argument" will start again (one week? one month? one hour?)
```

_(reply depth: 15)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>

Leif Svalgaard wrote in message <3658adf1.0@news1.ibm.net>...
>William M. Klein wrote in message <73a5f6$643@dfw-ixnews6.ix.netcom.com>...
>>A) The EXIT PARAGRAPH/SECTION syntax being introduced into the next
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>>problems (and they do exist) that this new syntax will allow and whether
or
>>not they should be included.  These threads convince me that they really
>are
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I don't mean to cause concern, but some of the problems with the new EXIT
formats are:

1) What happens if you EXIT SECTION from a paragraph that was PERFORMED from
outside that section?

2) What happens if you EXIT PARAGRAPH from a paragraph that was reached via
GO TO (and - for example - that paragraph is the last paragraph in your
source code)?

3) What happens when you EXIT some of the "strange to reach" procedures such
as Input/Output procedures?  Use Declaratives?

Just to be clear, these are all places where the current Standard and syntax
CAN cause your problems with GO TO, overlapping PERFORMs, etc - but there
was some discussion and concern in the committee about adding NEW ways for a
bad (or "trying to be tricky") programmer to get in trouble.
```

```
Bill,
I've added my suggestion for resolving the problem with EXITs to each item
of your list below:

William M. Klein wrote in message <73adri$92l@sjx-ixn9.ix.netcom.com>...
>Leif Svalgaard wrote in message <3658adf1.0@news1.ibm.net>...
>>William M. Klein wrote in message
<73a5f6$643@dfw-ixnews6.ix.netcom.com>...
>>>A) The EXIT PARAGRAPH/SECTION syntax being introduced into the next
>>Standard
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>1) What happens if you EXIT SECTION from a paragraph that was PERFORMED
from
>outside that section?

If a paragraph in a section is performed out-of-section, EXIT SECTION should
work
as EXIT PARAGRAPH. I assume that EXIT SECTION also exits the current
paragraph
in the normal situation, where you performed the section, and then exits the
section.

>
>2) What happens if you EXIT PARAGRAPH from a paragraph that was reached via
>GO TO (and - for example - that paragraph is the last paragraph in your
>source code)?

the same as if you had reached the end of the paragraph by executing the
rest of it.
the last paragraph is a non-issue as you have that problem even without EXIT
PARAGRAPH
>
>3) What happens when you EXIT some of the "strange to reach" procedures
such
>as Input/Output procedures?  Use Declaratives?

the same as if you reached the end the normal way.

>
>Just to be clear, these are all places where the current Standard and
syntax
>CAN cause your problems with GO TO, overlapping PERFORMs, etc - but there
>was some discussion and concern in the committee about adding NEW ways for
a
>bad (or "trying to be tricky") programmer to get in trouble.


*Every* addition to the standard is a new way for that programmer to get in
trouble,
and that price is worth it if we can get rid of the GO TO xxx-EXIT disease.
```

```
'Twas Sun, 22 Nov 1998 17:25:58 -0800, when "William M. Klein"
<wmklein@ix.netcom.com> illuminated comp.lang.cobol thusly:

>1) What happens if you EXIT SECTION from a paragraph that was PERFORMED from
>outside that section?

That's a good one.  Have you asked J4 to rule on that one?  As I read CD
1.4 p 438, 14.10.13.3 GR 12 this is a branch out of the PERFORMed
paragraph.

>
>2) What happens if you EXIT PARAGRAPH from a paragraph that was reached via
>GO TO (and - for example - that paragraph is the last paragraph in your
>source code)?

You fall into the next paragraph.  If there's no next paragraph you fall
into p 386 14.7.2 which states "the flow of control is undefined unless"
it is defined.

>3) What happens when you EXIT some of the "strange to reach" procedures such
>as Input/Output procedures?  Use Declaratives?

All the usual fall-off-the-bottom logic applies.

>Just to be clear, these are all places where the current Standard and syntax
>CAN cause your problems with GO TO, overlapping PERFORMs, etc - but there
>was some discussion and concern in the committee about adding NEW ways for a
>bad (or "trying to be tricky") programmer to get in trouble.

All tools are dangerous.

William M. Klein wrote in message <73a5f6$643@dfw-ixnews6.ix.netcom.com>...
>I just thought that I would jump in here with a comment on this (and the
>almost monthly similar) threads.  Some things they point out to me are:
>
[...]
>
>OK - folks,
>  Have I missed any of the alternatives?

CALLs to nested programs as an alternative for PERFORM THRU.
The EXIT PROGRAM statement replaces the GO TO exit-paragraph.
It has the added feature of localizing some data items.

>  Does anyone really think that
>someone from one camp is going to EVER convince people in another camp?

Although it may be rare for an opinion to be reversed by such
discussions, a modification of that opinion is possible. The views
expressed may also help those who have not yet taken a position
on the subject.

Each resurrection of these subjects may bring some new ideas that
were not heard previously. Old ideas may be expressed in a new form
that adds insight.

As a result of thinking about some comments made in an earlier part
of the thead, it occurred to me that nested programs might be a
reasonable alternative to PERFORM THRU. I spent several hours
yesterday investigating the use of nested programs and in modifying
a toy program that I use for such purposes. Although I have not yet
reached a decision to use nested programs, it looks promising.

Rick,
  I don't know which compiler (and OS) you are using, but be careful with
nested programs.  There are some compilers for which this is a REAL serious
performance degrader (compared to PERFORM).  There are others for which this
is a perfectly reasonable performing variation.  Personally, I wouldn't like
to have to analyze all my data to see which should be in local programs,
which in containing programs and which need to be global.  You also need to
be careful of the structure of your application.  Depending on how many
times and places your application code is called from, you may well need to
understand and use "COMMON" programs.  All of this is do-able, but is
certainly NOT the way that I would go to avoid structured vs unstructured
logic problems.

the presence of GO TO!

phase in the PROGRAM-ID paragraph. It is my intention, for this program,
to convert all paragraphs to nested programs eliminating all but inline
PERFORMs. When I have finished, I will determine what kind of hell
I have created.

in my case, with this program, it is relatively simple because I have some
tools to help and the program is not very large.

One objection that I thought of this morning is the standard requires
that EXIT PROGRAM be in its own paragraph; hence, any nested
program which needs to be ANSI 85 could still use GO TO xxxx-EXIT.
The proposed standard apparently removes this restriction.

```

Howard Brazee wrote in message <36597AE7.A6358ED8@home.com>...
>
>Maybe he was referring to multiple COBOL programs in one compile.  In
>that case, your reply says everything.  But externally compiled calls
>can add to maintenance troubles.
>
Not external - ANS 85 nested calls.

The format is similar to:

program-id. main-pgm.
01 global-data global.
01 main-local-data.
procedure division.
    call "nested-1"
    call "nested-2"
    stop run
    .

program-id. nested-1 is common.
01 nested-1-local-data.
procedure division.
    call "nested-3"
    exit program
    .
end program nested-1.

program-id. nested-2 is common.
01 nested-2-local-data.
procedure division.
    call "nested-3"
    exit program
    .
end program nested-2.

program-id. nested-3 is common.
01 nested-3-local-data.
procedure division.
    exit program
    .
end program nested-3.

end program main-pgm.

Nesting is to one level only.
-------------------------------
Rick Smith
e-mail: < ricksmith@aiservices.com >
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EXIT PARAGRAPH, PERFORM...THRU etc. (was: First COBOL compiler)

_(reply depth: 14)_

- **From:** John <johnhicks@ibm.net>
- **Date:** 1998-11-26T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<365E1297.6359D1AF@ibm.net>`
- **References:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk> <19981119102634.09602.00000036@ng-cr1.aol.com> <Tpc52.297$Sf1.435979@news3.mia> <3655866a.0@news3.uswest.net> <lSl52.587$Gy.710084@news4.mia> <3655f11c.0@news1.ibm.net> <OmB52.1091$Sf1.1090000@news3.mia> <3656ed2c.0@news1.ibm.net> <36579F79.329A@swbell.net> <36586f1b.0@news1.ibm.net> <73a5f6$643@dfw-ixnews6.ix.netcom.com>`

```
Thanks, William M. Klein, for jumping in when you did. Your analysis
correctly reduces the various holy wars over specific COBOL constructs
to three different programming styles, any one of which can lead to well
structured programs if used consistently.

I would summarize them a little more succinctly (and in a different
order):

A. Perform only single paragraphs. Do not use GO TO or sections.

B. Perform only paragraph ranges (using PERFORM THRU), using a
consistent naming convention for the exit paragraph of the range. Use a
GO TO only to go to the exit paragraph of the current performed range.
Do not use sections.

C. Perform only single sections. Use GO TO only to paragraphs within the
same section.

A key point is that whichever style is chosen, it must be used
consistently in a given program (and preferably in a given programming
shop).

The advocates of Style A are GO-TO-less programmers in the tradition of
Dikstra and Hoare. The result is an easy-to-follow subroutine structure,
but (IMHO) often awkward structures within subroutines. The
EXIT-procedure statement will help, but an EXIT-structure statement
would be better.

Judson McClendon makes a good argument for Style B (and this style seems
the most prevalent in the shops I've been in lately). This option still
allows for GO-TO-less programming, but retains the option of an
exceptional GO-TO out of a structure to a subroutine exit. Judson
strengthens the case for this style by advocating a preprocessor to
check for compliance.

I support Style C because it combines the advantages of A and B: It
results in an easy-to-follow subroutine structure (by avoiding PERFORM
THRUs), and it preserves the option of using exceptional GO-TOs to an
exit paragraph. Of the three styles, it is the least restrictive, since
it allows for other GO TOs than simply ones to a single exit. That may
raise some flags in some minds, but I submit a good case can be made for
preserving a little flexibility:

For example, each section in my programs always has three exit
paragraphs, each of which sets one of three exit conditions (normal,
exceptional, and error). This may seem like overkill to some, but I
believe my adopting this simple convention  improved the quality of my
code more than any other single step I've taken in my 26 years of COBOL
coding. 

I also occasionally find the PERFORM/END-PERFORM structure to be awkward
for a loop structure and so I resort to a set of LOOP and EXIT-LOOP
paragraphs and corresponding GO TOs. (An EXIT-PERFORM statement would
eliminate the need for this, but apparently it has been dropped from the
new standard.)

I don't mean for the two preceding paragraphs to start two new religious
battles in this ongoing war, but my point (at the moment) is that the
flexibility of Style C might make it more practical for use in a large
programming shop where minor deviations from the official style are are
inevitable.

Like Mr. McClendon, I too have a preprocessor and set of utilities that
help me enforce my style. (I even have a program that converts styles A
and B to Style C!) 

Yes, these ideas have been the topic of many an endless thread on this
news group for years.  But the subject at the heart of it all -- good
programming structure -- is an important one and is worthy of careful
analysis (there is no other kind of analysis). Again, I thank Mr. Klein
for cutting through the rhetoric and getting down to some much needed
analysis.

--John Hicks

It strike me that both Judson and Leif have a particular bias
in their styles, which I think they would both agree with. The
styles that they espouse are particularly suited to automated
code production and easily *generated* code.

Periods, in fact, are only difficult to deal with for a computer.
The eye handles them so well that every single written human
language has used the dot as a primary punctuation method.
To a computer, they are one of 256 (or 128) byte types. To
the eye, they are the smallest mark. The eye will scan a paragraph,
and parse by dots, far faster than it will span a page and pick
out five types of end-something's among a cluster of hyphenated
words.

Perfectly worded code could be justified by paragraph and
be as readable as a raggedy assed indentation method designed
to make up in appearance for lack of structure and coherence.

Just to illustrate my point, not one person uses case to document,
even though every human language does. All the code samples
were all one case.  Easy for the machine to process.

>>

_(reply depth: 16)_

- **From:** rkrayhawk@aol.com (RKRayhawk)

```

"Donald Tees" <donald@willmack.com>
Date: Fri, 27 Nov 1998 00:22:31 -0500

Made two comments that I hope I can take exception to without causing offense.
But let me hasten to say that I agree with your point that we are each fairly
comfortable with our own style. And I admire your distillation of the
discussion into a few concise points of view.

But let me note two things because they are interesting (I hope) and in a small
way relate to how we communicate on the source code screen.

Each quote is extracted with heavy snipping:

<<
...
Periods, in fact, are only difficult to deal with for a computer.
The eye handles them so well that every single written human
language has used the dot as a primary punctuation method.
...
>>

I am real sure that the latter part of that is not true. Writing materials have
been extremely precious resources at times. Truely ancient artifacts,
especially the picto-graph stage, do not reveal anything like a tendency
towards liberal spacing.  And the earliest use of alphabetic cipher systems
definitely had no space and no period.

This is especially true in text records.  Accounting records in Mycenaean times
do reveal usage of a colon-like character that functioned to indicate
apposition (I hope I used that term right), as in

sheep:5
cows:57

These records sometimes also used line breaks for account separation. Early on
they really had no period and it _was_ Greek.

They might have considered our use of spaces and periods a bit wimpy, and I am
not sure that they could have understood an EXIT of anykind. Early writing was
fallthru, whether it was left to right, right to left or boustrophedon.

At another point you "Donald Tees" 
states
<<
...
Just to illustrate my point, not one person uses case to document,
even though every human language does. All the code samples
were all one case.  Easy for the machine to process.

>>

Your statement is not completely clear to me, so forgive me if the following is
too far off the mark.  I am understanding it to mean that we are not using
upper case / lower case in our examples and/or that computer languages do not
use it. And I don't mean to jump on your phrasing, it is just that there is
something to deal with here that has a universal relevance. And I would like to
amplify your point that I think I agree with.

It is surely the case that we could over-document our meaning.  That is, we
could invent languages, or styles and policies that are way to detailed in
representing our instructions to the computer.  And I think we are in agreement
that when you get down to upper/lower case transitions, we are at or near the
point of no benefits.

If I understand your thoughts, I think I actually disagree with one factual
assertion. Actually there is atleast one standard in place that uses shifts in
case to document: and I consider it a disaster. This is, the Hungarian
Notation. 

This convention attempts to document the 'type' of variables while allowing you
to document there purpose.

It is popular in C environments and with modern COBOL compilers could be used
in COBOL as well.

If we are to document our data types in there name why not go back to the
jungle and use BASICs $, % and # signs. And since with C++ you can establish
your own types with classes, isn't it impossible to come up with enough
conventions.

Yet, ... and I do mean right at this point we get the trade off, ... it is
arguable that 

  TheDataName 

is atleast as effective as 

  THE-DATA-NAME

in visual communication.

So I suppose that we could erect a fence somewhere out here and see who is a
Case Shifter and see who is a Solid, who is adept at straddling this particular
fence.  But my point is that we do not need so many graphical tools.

And as a consequence of admiring your distillation style, "Donald Tees",
I shall try to mimick it. 

We can augment the visual interface of text presentation with several small
scale graphical enhancements.  Among these, in decreasing order of significance
are

1) procedure breaks
2) line breaks
3) indentation
4) coloration (HILITE)
4) alphabetic case shifting
5) the dreaded punctuation marks.

These seem to interplay rather naturally with scope initiators and scope
terminators.

As a consequence of using these graphical conventions, the apparent shape of
the text becomes an actual representation of the meaning of the text, but only
generally.

And as we attempt to be more specific in our indications, the graphical
deployments become less effective, because they are general and not specific.

Since I chopped up the original post for my own puposes I'll include it all
here in deference to the original postor.

"Donald Tees" <donald@willmack.com>
Date: Fri, 27 Nov 1998 00:22:31 -0500

- **From:** "Leif Svalgaard" <leif@ibm.net>

```
RKRayhawk wrote in message <19981127233604.01935.00001640@ng154.aol.com>...
>"Donald Tees" <donald@willmack.com>
>>Just to illustrate my point, not one person uses case to document,
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>representing our instructions to the computer.  And I think we are in
agreement
>that when you get down to upper/lower case transitions, we are at or near
the
>point of no benefits.
>
>If I understand your thoughts, I think I actually disagree with one factual
>assertion. Actually there is atleast one standard in place that uses shifts
in
>case to document: and I consider it a disaster. This is, the Hungarian
>Notation.
>
>This convention attempts to document the 'type' of variables while allowing
you
>to document there purpose.
>
>It is popular in C environments and with modern COBOL compilers could be
used
>in COBOL as well.
>

Hungarian Notation was invented by an old colleague of mine, Karoly Simony,
and
*can* be used properly, namely as we do in Cobol already by prefixing a
data-name
with its context (the prefix we often see in copybooks). It was never meant
to be used
to denote simple datatypes like 'int' or 'long'. It *is* used by C-coders in
this wrong
way and sometimes leads to disastrous results. I have seen a variable
'intTemp'
actually being a long (because it overflowed during testing), but the name
was never
changed (guess what: there was already a 'longTemp').
Even in straight Cobol, I have seen horrors like this:
  05  FILLER5   PIC X(6)  VALUE SPACES.
Where the size (5) was a part of the name, but where later maintenance
changed
the actual size to 6.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EXIT PARAGRAPH, PERFORM...THRU etc. (was: First COBOL compiler)

_(reply depth: 17)_

- **From:** "Donald Tees" <donald@willmack.com>
- **Date:** 1998-11-28T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<73p4an$b1m$1@news.igs.net>`
