```
Hell, I never take offence ... at least hardly ever <g>.

RKRayhawk wrote in message <19981127233604.01935.00001640@ng154.aol.com>...

With regard to "dots" as puctuation, I was not thinking of
it being used in the same way as we use it.  Hebrew, for
example, used various types of dots to add the vowels.
Most of the pictograph type languages use dots of various
sorts to modify the word, rather than the sentence.

Regardless.  The point is that the point is very close
to "white space", also seen easily.  Can you imagine
using, for example, a printable character instead
of the space for delimiting words?  The "." bears an
"eye tag" close to that of white space.  That was my
point.  Specific words do not.

>Just to illustrate my point, not one person uses case to document,
>even though every human language does. All the code samples
>were all one case.  Easy for the machine to process.

Let me say right off the bat that I do not want case sensitive
code.  However, the fact that the computer treats both cases
the same, while capitalized words stand out to the eye like
flashing lights is an interesting fact that could well be used
to make maintenance easier.  Lets say, for example, that
I used capitalization on all the IF and ELSES, rather than
indentation to make them stand out.  Or perhaps I capitalize
all words in a paragraph that effect flow control.

It is no different than the use of colour by editors for
reserved words ... meaningfull only to the programmer,
not to the compiler.  If we are talking stylistic components,
ignoring the use of case to add readability seems to be
wastefull.

My point was that no one has explored such mechanisms
in this disscussion.  The methods being discussed, in all
cases, effectively require the human programmer to act
more like a computer, rather than requiring the computer
language to act more like a human.

That, I think, is the crux of my arguements.  I want to evolve
a style that is clear to other humans.  It is the compiler's job
to make it clear to the computer.  The styles being espoused
make it easy for the human to think like a computer, not the
other way arround.  Maybe that is the best we can do.

I wrote a program last night, in which I used a completely
new style for myself.  No white space, no final period
for a paragraph, no exit paragraph.  Since I use no GOTO's
(or perform thru's) the
end of a paragraph *is* clearly delimited ... by the paragraph
name of the next paragraph.  If there are no go to's, and
flow *NEVER* proceeds to the next paragraph but *ALWAYS*
returns to the perform statement, then the traditional white
space between a "performed paragraph" and an inline
paragraph is complety meaningless, and takes up the
vertical bandwidth on the screen that you talked about earlier.

The code looks "busy" to my eye without white space. It does
seem to be easier to work on, though.  It is quite a bit smaller
on the screen.

If all three of the styles ("exit-paragraph", single dot on a line by
itself, and final period followed by a blank line) are used simply
to clearly delimit the end of a statement, then they are  just noise.
The exit statement is only usefull if you use it.

A new paragraph name does the same equally effectively
PROVIDED that one does not use goto's.  Ever.  I may need the
odd scope delimiter to accomlish that, but the period will do it
quite effectively 99% of the time.

>As a consequence of using these graphical conventions, the apparent shape
of
>the text becomes an actual representation of the meaning of the text, but
only
>generally.


This is what bothers me, actually.  We are saying that the text
is meaningfull to the computer, while adding constructs that are
making it harder for the human to read,  while the shape of code
on the screen the screen tells the human what it means, but is
ignored by the computer.  When such things happen in human
languages, we call it cultural bias, and know that it leads to
mistakes.  Good style should be readable by both audiences.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EXIT PARAGRAPH, PERFORM...THRU etc. (was: First COBOL compiler)

_(reply depth: 18)_

- **From:** "Warren G. Simmons" <warrens@inficad.com>
- **Date:** 1998-11-28T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<36607945.4E208AD3@inficad.com>`
- **References:** `<73lcf6$76n$1@news.igs.net> <19981127233604.01935.00001640@ng154.aol.com> <73p4an$b1m$1@news.igs.net>`

```
An aside to this interesting debate is more of my folklore.

Programs in the 1950-60 period were written in machine, assembly, octal,
etc. As close to the machine as one could get was required because
coding tools of all kinds were very young. After half a decade of code
creation and documentation, nothing really existed to define
what the program did UNLESS you did something extra.  The tendency was
NOT to do the extra.

Bosses, being the same as they have always been were very frustrated
by the answers they got when they requested changes, and IT staff could
NOT shed any light on where, how, what, and when those changes could
occur.  Some of these bosses had come up through the unit record card
type systems, and had wired boards. Some asked to see the programs.
Guess what they were shown. As much as any other reason, COBOL was
and still is intended to help everyone KNOW what is going on including
the IT people. The fact that some of you have developed your own
preprocessors to enforce your own quality control seems to support the
need.

One more point. I prefer the use of ENGLISH rules. Not that I'm good
with them, but that it is easier to pick them apart. One of the very
nice improvements has been the introduction of the Evaluate statement.
It's ENGLISH, but easier to understand than nested IF statements, and in
similar to GO TO Depending on.  In ENGLISH new words are created all of
the time. However, seldom are they created in the form END-IF, etc.

Warren Simmons
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EXIT PARAGRAPH, PERFORM...THRU etc. (was: First COBOL compiler)

_(reply depth: 19)_

- **From:** "Donald Tees" <donald@willmack.com>
- **Date:** 1998-11-28T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<73pkhe$j6j$1@news.igs.net>`
- **References:** `<73lcf6$76n$1@news.igs.net> <19981127233604.01935.00001640@ng154.aol.com> <73p4an$b1m$1@news.igs.net> <zFU72.7817$w_6.4905358@news2.mia>`

```
Judson McClendon wrote in message ...

>Donald, I'm having a hard time visualizing what you mean by 'no white
>space'.  We're talking COBOL, right?  Surely you must have white space
>between tokens.  Do you mean putting sentences consecutively on a line,
>like this paragraph?  Could you give us an example paragraph?


THIS-IS-PARAGRAPH-ONE.
        THIS IS LINE ONE.
        THIS IS LINE TWO.
THIS-IS-PARAGRAPH-TWO.
         THIS IS LINE ONE OF THIS-IS-PARAGRAPH-TWO.
*This would be white space if it had no comment.
THIS-IS-PARAGRAPGH-THREE.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EXIT PARAGRAPH, PERFORM...THRU etc. (was: First COBOL compiler)

_(reply depth: 15)_

- **From:** "Michael C. Kasten" <mck9@swbell.net>
- **Date:** 1998-11-27T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<365EB29F.12DD@swbell.net>`
- **References:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk> <19981119102634.09602.00000036@ng-cr1.aol.com> <Tpc52.297$Sf1.435979@news3.mia> <3655866a.0@news3.uswest.net> <lSl52.587$Gy.710084@news4.mia> <3655f11c.0@news1.ibm.net> <OmB52.1091$Sf1.1090000@news3.mia> <3656ed2c.0@news1.ibm.net> <36579F79.329A@swbell.net> <36586f1b.0@news1.ibm.net> <73a5f6$643@dfw-ixnews6.ix.netcom.com> <365E1297.6359D1AF@ibm.net>`

```
John wrote:

[snip -- enumerating coding styles] 

> C. Perform only single sections. Use GO TO only to paragraphs within the
> same section.

[snip]

> I support Style C because it combines the advantages of A and B: It
> results in an easy-to-follow subroutine structure (by avoiding PERFORM
> THRUs), and it preserves the option of using exceptional GO-TOs to an
> exit paragraph. Of the three styles, it is the least restrictive, since
> it allows for other GO TOs than simply ones to a single exit.

[snip]

You evidently regard "least restrictive" as a virtue.  I regard it
as a vice.  Code is easiest to read and understand when there is the
least uncertainty about what happens next.

In the absence of GO TO, I can look at a paragraph in isolation,
knowing that it will always return control to whoever PERFORMed it.
In the presence of GO TO I can't be sure.  When PERFORMed it returns 
control to the PERFORMer; otherwise it falls into the next paragraph.
I can't look at a paragraph in isolation -- I must look at all the 
code which invokes it, and at the paragraph following it.

I would summarize your recommendation as follows: if you serve up 
spaghetti on small enough plates, you'll have some hope of untangling 
it.

Michael C. Kasten	mck9@swbell.net
http://home.swbell.net/mck9/cobol/cobol.html
```

_(reply depth: 17)_

- **From:** "Michael C. Kasten" <mck9@swbell.net>
- **Date:** 1998-11-30T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<36634331.6EDD@swbell.net>`
- **References:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk> <19981119102634.09602.00000036@ng-cr1.aol.com> <Tpc52.297$Sf1.435979@news3.mia> <3655866a.0@news3.uswest.net> <lSl52.587$Gy.710084@news4.mia> <3655f11c.0@news1.ibm.net> <OmB52.1091$Sf1.1090000@news3.mia> <3656ed2c.0@news1.ibm.net> <36579F79.329A@swbell.net> <36586f1b.0@news1.ibm.net> <73a5f6$643@dfw-ixnews6.ix.netcom.com> <365E1297.6359D1AF@ibm.net> <365EB29F.12DD@swbell.net> <3662BBC5.15280394@home.com>`

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> 
> > In the absence of GO TO, I can look at a paragraph in isolation,
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> paragraph without screwing up!

Very true -- my summary was an oversimplification.  In order for
paragraphs to be understandable in isolation, it is not enough that 
there be no GO TO.  There must also be no PERFORM THRU, and the first
paragraph must exit the program (directly or indirectly) before falling 
into the next one.  If there are multiple entry points, the last 
condition applies to each entry point.

The first two conditions, if true, can be quickly verified in any
reasonable editor.  The third may take a little longer, but usually
doesn't.

If these conditions are NOT true, then it can be a long, tedious,
and error prone exercise to reliably identify all the fall-through
code.  We skip this analysis at our peril.

Michael C. Kasten	mck9@swbell.net
http://home.swbell.net/mck9/cobol/cobol.html
```

- **Date:** 1998-12-01T00:00:00

```
Michael C. Kasten wrote:
>
>Very true -- my summary was an oversimplification.  In order for
>paragraphs to be understandable in isolation, it is not enough that
>there be no GO TO.  There must also be no PERFORM THRU, ... [snip]


I must protest! :-)  After some 20+ years of managing and teaching new
and experienced programmers, I can tell you that there is *no* problem
with newbies understanding and using PERFORM/THRU, as long as every
paragraph is paired with exactly one EXIT paragraph, and you never,
ever, never GO TO out of a paragraph (that's a firing offense with me).
Some programmers here don't like it for various reasons, but I have
never seen a single problem with a programmer misunderstanding it, or
finding it difficult to modify. :-)  Yes, without an automated checker
it presents opportunities for the PERFORM and THRU paragraphs to be
mismatched, but that is a keying problem, not an understanding problem.
```

_(reply depth: 19)_

- **From:** "Michael C. Kasten" <mck9@swbell.net>
- **Date:** 1998-12-01T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3663DE5B.705D@swbell.net>`
- **References:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk> <19981119102634.09602.00000036@ng-cr1.aol.com> <Tpc52.297$Sf1.435979@news3.mia> <3655866a.0@news3.uswest.net> <lSl52.587$Gy.710084@news4.mia> <3655f11c.0@news1.ibm.net> <OmB52.1091$Sf1.1090000@news3.mia> <3656ed2c.0@news1.ibm.net> <36579F79.329A@swbell.net> <36586f1b.0@news1.ibm.net> <73a5f6$643@dfw-ixnews6.ix.netcom.com> <365E1297.6359D1AF@ibm.net> <365EB29F.12DD@swbell.net> <3662BBC5.15280394@home.com> <36634331.6EDD@swbell.net> <qKQ82.16090$Gy.6464295@news4.mia>`

```
Judson McClendon wrote:
> 
> Michael C. Kasten wrote:
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> mismatched, but that is a keying problem, not an understanding problem.

The key here is my use of the phrase "in isolation".

Even with a disciplined THRU...EXIT style, you can't understand a
paragraph in isolation, in a formal sense.  You can only understand
a real paragraph together with its matching EXIT paragraph.  You
have to understand, and either assume or confirm, that the target of
the GO TO is a trivial EXIT paragraph which does nothing substantive.

Any use of PERFORM THRU, by definition, involves fall-through logic,
even if it is of a trivial sort.  Whether such a style is desirable
is a different issue.  It remains true that in the presence of
fall-through logic you can't understand the program without
recognizing the fall-throughs, whether the recognition is performed
by a code checker or by your eyeballs.

Michael C. Kasten	mck9@swbell.net
http://home.swbell.net/mck9/cobol/cobol.html
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Ersatz EXITs

_(reply depth: 20)_

- **From:** rkrayhawk@aol.com (RKRayhawk)
- **Date:** 1998-12-02T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<19981201231110.26237.00000075@ng101.aol.com>`
- **References:** `<3663DE5B.705D@swbell.net>`

```
docdwarf@clark.net ()
On 1 Dec 1998 15:23:29 GMT
posted a  pocedure that takes an exit stage left when a severe error is
encountered, which looks like this after heavy snipping
<<
...
A100-HOUSEKEEPING.
     OPEN INPUT INFILE.
     IF NOT GOOD-INFILE-IO
         ...
         GO TO Z999-ALL-FALL-DOWN.
...
>>
the target procedure Z999-ALL-FALL-DOWN is outside of the PERFORM THRU range.
And the discussion that has followed is interesting. I would like to offer a
term for this topic, and have pirated the subject paradigmatically.

I suggest that we call this coding technique an 'Ersatz EXIT'. This is in fact
one of the most proven valid exceptions to the 'no goto out of' rule.

But notice that I do not recommend that we call it 'Ersatz GOTO'. I would not
recommend that we encourage people to use flow disruptive GOTOs in COBOL. But
as one postor commented, sometimes you HAVE TO abend.

The technique of GOING to an abend paragraph is very, very, very common. It is
a pedagogical error, I emphasize ERROR, to teach it not.

Furthermore, this ubiquitous coding technique, is exactly analogous to the use
of procedure vector table devices, such as CICS HANDLE CONDITION. A device that
many people depend on heavily.

COBOLers are not dumb. They are perfectly capable of understanding a procedur
vector table and an asynchronous engagement of it.  COBOLers ar perfectly
capable of understanding GO TO ABEND, that ain't rocket science.

But my notion would be to teach this as an EXIT technique.  Someday we could
enforce it with syntax in the compiler.  Rather than GOTO we should EXIT TO
Z999-ALL-FALL-DOWN.  Such syntax would be slightly more clear, but more
importantly the compiler could verify that that Z999- procedure contains
certain death, or your code fails compilation.

The need for Ersatz EXITs is clear, it is large, and there is no holding it
back, no matter what theory we profess. The code is out there and it works
quite well. But there are actually cases where you can get into trouble with
this.  Abend paragraphs don't always abend, for example.

I have seen CICS programs that had Ersatz EXITs, that worked for a while. There
were the GOTOs, and the paragraph actually abended in the begining.  But then
some one modified it to take a snap dump and return. The code went to
production.  The coding weakness was not recognized in test because the ideal
data conditions never triggered the Ersatz EXIT.  One night it happened, and
then the code blithely dropped into the next paragraph, and then the next.

The problem was that several of the fallen through paragraphs actually
functioned leaving many confusing side effects.  It was a bear to decipher.

So in the absence of compiler support for validation of the 
certainty of the abend, Ersatz EXITs _are_ dangerous, and you could see how
structured programming adherents would rail against them.  But they are
efficient devices, as measured in programmer time.

I find Thane Hubbell comments on the possible use of PERFORM instead of GOTO in
these cases somewhat profoundly insightful: 

redsky@ibm.net (Thane Hubbell)
Date: 2 Dec 98 00:15:27 GMT
L

<<
I'm guilty of that --- but here' s my reasoning.

Sometimes you just HAVE to ABEND.  I PERFORM the ABEND routine.  Why? 
If I GO TO it, it defeats the optimization (the compiler WILL NOT 
optimize the program) and I lose runtime speed.  Using perform 
accomplishes two things - I get optimization (CALL directive with 
Realia), and I get rid of the GO TO <G>.
>>

Hope we can hear from other knowledgable folks about the other compilers and
the impact of the presence of GOTOs on the overall behaviour and quality of the
optimizer. As well as comments on the difficulty that the need to ABEND causes.

What is very hard to say in a few words is that if you do not have Ersatz
EXITs, then your top level structure becomes greatly burdened with artificial
material.  In a certain sense I advocate that, and I am close to Judson
McClendon view (except I don't like THRU and its retinue of wimpy GOTOs).

I have dealt with code that really does switch back and forth between PERFORM
and GOTO invocations of some paragraph sequences. It can be quite difficult to
modify it reliably, especially when adding procedures.

But truth is I have learned to be accepting of the Ersatz EXITs. Even in the
midst of code that is mangled, the Ersatz EXITs are not frequently a source of
problems.

Part of what we are dealing with is that a number of programs are not planned
well.  You really need a very good map ahead of time to get procedures
organized according to I/O status codes and severe exception conditions.  I am
not advocating inadequate planning, but I know that it is there frequently.

We frequently hand major programming responsibilities to individuals that do
not know how many exceptions can occur.  And we also make major revisions to
programs. The biggest source of difficulty here is a new file to be accessed in
the middle of a structure that was originally smooth.  A person is usually not
given permission to reorganize the program, they are just told to
open/read/close, "get it done, you don't have much time."

"What if, what if, what if," they will protest. And we frequently tell them,
don't worry, just GOTO the abend routine with a few identifying flags set.

Naturally there are many ways it can happen. But the field experience is that
in a very large set of cases the Ersatz EXIT in the form of a GOTO a reliable
abender, is valid when we do it at the surface level in source code or when we
allow engagement of error handling procedure vectors by default within major
support packages from vendors.

The stuff works, and I think we should teach it without condemnation so that we
might more successfully limit it.

Best Wishes,

Robert Rayhawk
RKRayhawk@aol.com
```

```
I'd tend to christen that sort of emergy exit an 'ABORT EXIT' rather than an

I've always thought that using a PERFORM ABORT-ROUTINE, which contains a

upon discovering an unrecoverable error.

big iron people at Big Blue?  I haven't programmed anything meant to run on
an IBM mainframe since I worked on a S/360 Model 65 (under OS/MVT?) back in
1969.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Ersatz EXITs

_(reply depth: 24)_

- **From:** "Donald Tees" <donald@willmack.com>

```
Dennis J. Minette wrote in message <#PQe$kjH#GA.251@upnetnews05>...
>I'm sure you meant to say that you include PERFORM ABSOLUTE-ABORT in your
>program coding quite frequently.  Somehow I just can't picture your

>actually exercising that functionality very often.  :-)


Thank you!<S>.
In all seriousness though, there are a lot of places in Cobol
where you have to code for errors that should not ever occur,
and if in fact they do, it is a coding bug.

Consider the following:

        read a-file invalid key
            perform create-new-record.

create-new-record.
        write a-file-record invalid key
            display "call Don and tell him xzy create new record does not
work"
            perform wait-for-keystroke
            stop run.

I often run into situations of that sort, and am never quite
sure how to code them. I prefer to code such that error
routines correct the error rather than report it, but attempting
to correct an error that should never happen ...
```

```
> I try to be more helpful to the person who has to deal with the problem so I
> would code the abort message in your example as:
> 
> MOVE ' DROP-THROUGH ERROR AT 2200-EXIT ' TO ERROR-MESSAGE.


That would work. 

Although the only case where I see this being a problem in most shops if
is where the standard is not to use sections, but there is an internal
sort. 

0000-MAIN SECTION.
0000-MAIN-PARAGRAPH.
    ...
0100-SORT-INPUT SECTION.
0100-SORT.
    ...
0200-SORT-OUTPUT SECTION.
0200-SORT.
    ...
1000-REST-OF-THE-PARAGRAPHS SECTION.
1000-PARAGRAPH-1.
    ...
2000-PARAGRAPH-2.

That paranoid paragraph can catch drop throughs to MAIN-PARAGRAPH, but
that paragraph typically ends with a GOBACK or STOP RUN.  If someone
sticks a paragraph in one of the sort sections, we're out of luck.  I
guess trick here is to make the SORT paragraphs only a line long each.
e.g.
0100-SORT-INPUT SECTION.
0100-SORT.
    PERFORM 1100-SORT-IN.
0200-SORT-OUTPUT SECTION.
0200-SORT.
    PERFORM 1200-SORT-OUT.
1000-REST-OF-THE-PARAGRAPHS SECTION.
1100-SORT-IN.
    ...
1200-SORT-OUT.
    ...

And use an optimizer!!
```

```
Donald Tees wrote in message <744mdo$9qm$1@news.igs.net>...

>Dennis J. Minette wrote in message <#PQe$kjH#GA.251@upnetnews05>...

>
Donald,

I believe you are doing the right thing here.  I also do the same sort of
thing.  Sort of a belt and suspenders approach to defensive coding for
"things that logically should not happen."  Being sometimes a perfectionist
to a paranoid fault, I include within the data displayed to the operator
(and/or saved in an error file) additional salient information such as key
of record that failed to write, key of prior record written, etc.  Anything
that in my judgment would help to pinpoint the cause of "the event that
should not have occurred at all."  I have encountered more of those sort of
events than I care to remember, and every one of them strengthened my
resolve to be more diligent in trying to anticipate them.  Since I am the
logical one to be called if some of my good code goes bad, I like to start
the investigation of the problem with as much as possible of the information
that was available only at "abort time."
```

```

Howard Brazee wrote in message <3667F451.E506658F@home.com>...
>I would love to be able to never code a SECTION again in my life.  Are
>there compilers which allow a sort to use paragraphs instead of
>sections?

Any compiler that conforms to the '85 Standard allows SORT INPUT/OUTPUT
procedures to be paragraphs rather than sections.  (This was a substantive
change from the '74 Standard).  Only DECLARATIVES still require SECTIONS.
```

```
On Fri, 04 Dec 1998 07:40:17 -0700, Howard Brazee <NOSPAMbrazee@home.com> wrote:

>I would love to be able to never code a SECTION again in my life.  Are
>there compilers which allow a sort to use paragraphs instead of
>sections?

I'm not entirely certain this is what you're looking for but the Acucobol-GT
docs offer the following (trimmed liberally):

SORT sort-file 
	[...]
  { INPUT PROCEDURE IS proc-name  }

  { OUTPUT PROCEDURE IS proc-name } 
	[...]

proc-name has the following format

	start-proc [(THRU|THROUGH) end-proc]

"Start-proc and end-proc are paragraph or section names in the Procedure
Division."


# Chris Adams <cadams@acucorp.com>
```

```
In article <743hun$ieg$1@news.igs.net>,
Donald Tees <donald@willmack.com> wrote:

[snippage]

>The GOBACK is also something I have never used.  As
>I understand GOBACK, it is an if called then exit else if
>executed then stop instruction.  Is that correct?
>
>Dennis J. Minette wrote in message <#f3I6BeH#GA.142@upnetnews05>...

[snippage]

>>BTW, I've never used a GO BACK construct.  Is that something invented by the
>>big iron people at Big Blue?  I haven't programmed anything meant to run on
>>an IBM mainframe since I worked on a S/360 Model 65 (under OS/MVT?) back in
>>1969.

It is gratifying to see a thread about something which can generate such
*passion* as GO TO-less programming proceed with such grace and reason...
be that as it may my apologies for causing confusions with the GOBACK.

I am not aware if GOBACK is a Big Blue extension; as I understand it the
GOBACK returns execution to the previous program in the calling hierarchy
(said previous program can, of course, be the operating system) as cleanly
and neatly as can ever be expected to accomplish.

I was encouraged to use the GOBACK by the commone experience of 'ANYTHING
CAN HAPPEN'... in the formative experience a report was cobbled-together
to satisfy the rantings of a Corner-Office Idiot who needed it Just This
Once... and the report moved to Prod... and then someone was *so* jealous
of the report that he wanted something Just Like It (Only *Slightly*
Different)... and the one-time report got turned into a subroutine called
by another program... and it began to just... die in the middle of the
night.

No ABEND, nothing untoward... it just... died.  Since I had written the
original Q&D report I was asked to look into it and saw nothing wrong; I
then did what every Good Programmer did at such times in those days and
grabbed a cuppa coffee and a smoke.  Whilst thusly occupied I outlined my
difficulties to the shop's Old Man who was near the Javamatic, doing the
same as I... and he wandered over to my terminal, looked at the listing
and said 'Change that STOP RUN to a GOBACK.'

Ran like a watch after that, it did.

DD
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Ersatz EXITs

_(reply depth: 25)_

- **From:** "Leif Svalgaard" <leif@ibm.net>
- **Date:** 1998-12-02T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3665a0b6.0@news1.ibm.net>`
- **References:** `<3663DE5B.705D@swbell.net> <19981201231110.26237.00000075@ng101.aol.com> <#f3I6BeH#GA.142@upnetnews05> <743hun$ieg$1@news.igs.net> <743m6h$bim$1@callisto.clark.net> <743pbh$mls$1@news.igs.net>`

```
Donald Tees wrote in message <743pbh$mls$1@news.igs.net>...
>docdwarf@clark.net wrote in message <743m6h$bim$1@callisto.clark.net>...
>>difficulties to the shop's Old Man who was near the Javamatic, doing the
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>


The GOBACK has the same effect as the construction:

RETURN-TO-CALLER.
    EXIT PROGRAM
.
RETURN-TO-SYSTEM.
    STOP RUN
    .

If the program was called by another (Cobol?) program,
EXIT PROGRAM would return to that, otherwise EXIT
PROGRAM would have no effect and we would fall thru
(oh horror!) to the STOP RUN.

So, the GOBACK does not offer any new functionality,
but is rather only 'syntactic sugar' making things look a
bit nicer and more palatable.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Ersatz EXITs

_(reply depth: 24)_

- **From:** Barticus@att.spam.net (Randall Bart)
- **Date:** 1998-12-03T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<36665a7c.85029172@netnews>`
- **References:** `<3663DE5B.705D@swbell.net> <19981201231110.26237.00000075@ng101.aol.com> <#f3I6BeH#GA.142@upnetnews05> <743hun$ieg$1@news.igs.net> <743m6h$bim$1@callisto.clark.net>`

```
'Twas 2 Dec 1998 15:26:09 GMT, when docdwarf@clark.net () illuminated
comp.lang.cobol thusly:

>I am not aware if GOBACK is a Big Blue extension; as I understand it the
>GOBACK returns execution to the previous program in the calling hierarchy
>(said previous program can, of course, be the operating system) as cleanly
>and neatly as can ever be expected to accomplish.

GOBACK is an IBM extension, but someday (soon?) it won't be.  GOBACK is a
simple construct who's semantics boil down to "exit this program."  ANSI
COBOL-74 gave us the construct EXIT PROGRAM, which has semantics "exit
this program unless this program has the quality of outermostness."  One
cannot exit forever, there must be an outermost program level, but
shouldn't that be the kernel of the OS?  I've never seen a kernel written
in Cobol, but the standard has a construct which appears designed for
exactly that purpose.  So this quality of outermostness applies to the
outermost Cobol program, but what if Cobol program A calls Fortan program
B which calls Cobol program C?  Does EXIT PROGRAM in C return to B, or
leave you falling though in C?  Since Fortran is beyond the scope of the
Cobol standard, can it specify that behavior?  And if ANSI says EXIT
PROGRAM in Program C is a no-op, does this make it a useful construct for
anyone?  How does one exit back to B?

So Mr. Klein proposed that Cobol have a construct with the simple
semantics "exit this program", and since Big Blue had a construct of three
decades standing with these exact semantics, he chose their syntax.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Ersatz EXITs

_(reply depth: 24)_

- **From:** Howard Brazee <NOSPAMbrazee@home.com>
- **Date:** 1998-12-03T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3666C461.EEFAABC6@home.com>`
- **References:** `<3663DE5B.705D@swbell.net> <19981201231110.26237.00000075@ng101.aol.com> <#f3I6BeH#GA.142@upnetnews05> <743hun$ieg$1@news.igs.net> <743m6h$bim$1@callisto.clark.net>`

```
I never paid attention to how to exit a COBOL program.

In my first job, all programs exited with a "CALL EXIT".

Now jobs end with GOBACKs if they are called or STOP RUNs if they
aren't.  I didn't question why, all I knew is that they were standards
which didn't bother me.
```

```
    STOP RUN
```

```
Not exactly, because the above is invalid syntax (EXIT PROGRAM
must be in a paragraph by itself), so GOBACK is equivalent to:
RETURN-TO-CALLER.
      EXIT PROGRAM
      .
RETURN-TO-SYSTEM.
      STOP RUN
      .

>Speaking again in terms of the vaunted verbosity of COBOL, I believe I
>prefer the old, extended "Standard" version that GOBACK is equivalent to.
I
>can understand it's connotations and implications (or, at least, I _think_
I
>can.)
>
>We still haven't heard from anyone who can vouch for the legitimate birth
of
>GOBACK.

I don't know about 'birth' but if you substitute 'use', there actually is
(I just realized) a legitimate use. The above RETURN construction
requires a GO TO or a fall thru to work, so would not be equivalent
to GOBACK in (if you disallow GO TOs -cf. Thanes concern about
Realia Cobol's optimizer):

      IF A = B
            GOBACK
      ELSE
           .....
     .

because you just can't replace one with the other.

if
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Ersatz EXITs

- **Date:** 1998-12-03T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ubiZiWyH#GA.251@upnetnews05>`
- **References:** `<3663DE5B.705D@swbell.net> <19981201231110.26237.00000075@ng101.aol.com> <#f3I6BeH#GA.142@upnetnews05> <743hun$ieg$1@news.igs.net> <366578ff.167535743@news.acucorp.com> <OgabBljH#GA.222@upnetnews05> <3665b750.0@news1.ibm.net> <#AQE2UvH#GA.207@upnetnews05> <36671859.0@news1.ibm.net>`

```

Leif Svalgaard wrote in message <36671859.0@news1.ibm.net>...
>Dennis J. Minette wrote in message <#AQE2UvH#GA.207@upnetnews05>...
>>Leif Svalgaard wrote in message <3665b750.0@news1.ibm.net>...
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
>>>>prefer the old, extended "Standard" version that GOBACK is equivalent
to.
>>>>I can understand it's connotations and implications (or, at least, I
>_think_
>>>> Ican.)
>>>>
>>>>We still haven't heard from anyone who can vouch for the legitimate
birth
>of GOBACK.
>>>I don't know about 'birth' but if you substitute 'use', there actually is
…[32 more quoted lines elided]…
>wrong on this.


You _are_ wrong. :-)
How about the PROCEDURE DIVISION (DECLARATIVES omitted) must contain either:
zero sections, but one or more paragraphs
or
one or more sections, each containing one or more paragraphs.
(I don't believe an empty section would be any more kosher than an empty
paragraph, but I may be wrong on this.)


The use of SECTIONs and paragraphs are not mutually exclusive.  But if you
do use sections, you must start the PROCEDURE DIVISION with one.
BTW, I just cannot imagine a program with a PROCEDURE DIVISION that contains
_only_ SECTIONs and _no_ paragraphs.

>It would too high a price to pay for the
>GO-BACK section to have to use sections
>throughout.
>

My proposal would require only 2 sections.  A main-processing section at the
start of the procedure division, and my suggested GO-BACK section at the end
of the procedure division.

I have a tendency to use SECTIONs in COBOL programs sort of like bookmarks,
to mark the beginnings of important functional "groupings" of the processing
logic - in effect, creating functionally related groupings of paragraphs.
For instance, I usually corral the paragraphs for all data i/o routines
within a separate section of the procedure division, usually the last
section.  Just because you have sections in a program does not mean that
they ever have to actually be explicitly referenced by a GOTO or a PERFORM.
```

```

Donald Tees wrote in message <7457ho$ksj$2@news.igs.net>...
>
>Leif Svalgaard wrote in message <3665de26.0@news1.ibm.net>...
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>I have never had it rejected, or even flagged. Why illegal? I


Many compilers accept it, but the strict (Cobol 74) syntax says
that EXIT PROGRAM (just like the EXIT) must be in
a paragraph by itself, so
EXIT PROGRAM
STOP RUN
is illegal, while
RETURN-TO-CALLER.
    EXIT PROGRAM
    .
RETURN-TO-SYSTEM.
    STOP RUN
    .
is legal.
```

- **From:** redsky@ibm.net (Thane Hubbell)

- **Message-ID:** `<36675c8f.176597626@news1.ibm.net>`
- **References:** `<3663DE5B.705D@swbell.net> <19981201231110.26237.00000075@ng101.aol.com> <#f3I6BeH#GA.142@upnetnews05> <743hun$ieg$1@news.igs.net> <366578ff.167535743@news.acucorp.com> <OgabBljH#GA.222@upnetnews05> <3665b750.0@news1.ibm.net>`

```
On Wed, 2 Dec 1998 15:55:15 -0600, "Leif Svalgaard" <leif@ibm.net>
wrote:

>Dennis J. Minette wrote in message ...
>>Chris Adams wrote in message <366578ff.167535743@news.acucorp.com>...
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Actually, this is not true.  Exit Program CHANGED in ANSI 85 COBOL so
that it is no longer required to be in a paragraph alone.  I can
appear anywhere in the code.  If it is encountered in a main program
it is treated as a NO OP.  The only requirement is that if included in
an IMPERITIVE statement that it be the last statement.

Read Data-Record
   At End Exit Program
              Stop Run

Is invalid, However:

Perform The-Abend.

The-Abend.
    Exit Program
    Stop Run
    .

Is completely valid in Ansi 85 COBOL.
```

```
I think that we have different understandings of what an IMPERITIVE
statement is.  I did some research before I made the posting.  An
IMPERITIVE STATEMENT is a statement following an IMPERTIVE - simple
eh?

Impertives are:  On Exception
                         On Overflow
                         Invalid Key
                         At End
                         etc etc....

Statements within a paragraph are NOT imperitive statements.  In my
reading.  Is your definition different?

I would welcome comments from Don Nelson and Randall Barticus on what
an Imperitive statement is also.  (These two because they write COBOL
compilers).
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ GoBack (was Re: Ersatz EXITs

_(reply depth: 22)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 1998-12-02T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<743sa2$n28@sjx-ixn9.ix.netcom.com>`
- **References:** `<3663DE5B.705D@swbell.net> <19981201231110.26237.00000075@ng101.aol.com> <#f3I6BeH#GA.142@upnetnews05>`

```

Dennis J. Minette wrote in message <
>
>BTW, I've never used a GO BACK construct.  Is that something invented by
the
>big iron people at Big Blue?  I haven't programmed anything meant to run on
>an IBM mainframe since I worked on a S/360 Model 65 (under OS/MVT?) back in
>1969.

GoBack was originally an IBM mainframe extension.  It does an EXIT PROGRAM
in a subroutine but a Stop Run in a "main" routine (thus making it
unneccessary for the programmer to know in which way a specific module will
be used). This feature is included in the draft of the next COBOL Standard
(and is implemented as an extension in many existing mainframe and PC
compilers).

FYI,
  Some compilers allow the following syntax (which is illegal according to
the current Standard)

    do-something
      Exit Program
      Stop Run
   do-something-else

The current Standard does NOT allow an "Exit Program" to be followed by ANY
other imperative statement.  However, "logically" this is what a GoBack does
(because "Exit Program" is a no-op in a main program).
>
><<snipped the remaining appendage>>
>
>
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: GoBack (was Re: Ersatz EXITs

_(reply depth: 24)_

- **From:** "Bill Wood" <beavis27@email.msn.com>
- **Date:** 1998-12-08T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<OkUdOVxI#GA.293@upnetnews03>`
- **References:** `<3663DE5B.705D@swbell.net> <19981201231110.26237.00000075@ng101.aol.com> <#f3I6BeH#GA.142@upnetnews05> <743sa2$n28@sjx-ixn9.ix.netcom.com> <#1mOtUvH#GA.161@upnetnews05>`

```
We are using MicroFocus NetExpress V2.0 and have found that if we use an
EXIT PROGRAM to exit the program and then do a CANCEL after returning to the
calling program, the program is not actually cancelled.  When you call the
program the next time, all of the variables are in the state they were when
you last left the program.  We have had to replace all of our EXIT PROGRAM
statements with GOBACK to get the desired result of a CANCEL.

Just my two cents.

Bill
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: GoBack (was Re: Ersatz EXITs

_(reply depth: 25)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 1998-12-08T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<74l25s$plc@sjx-ixn10.ix.netcom.com>`
- **References:** `<3663DE5B.705D@swbell.net> <19981201231110.26237.00000075@ng101.aol.com> <#f3I6BeH#GA.142@upnetnews05> <743sa2$n28@sjx-ixn9.ix.netcom.com> <#1mOtUvH#GA.161@upnetnews05> <OkUdOVxI#GA.293@upnetnews03>`

```
Is there any chance that you are using the NODYNAM compiler directive?  That
is the only time that Micro Focus should work the way that you report.
(NODYNAM is a mainframe compatibility option to simulate what happens on IBM
mainframes when modules are statically link-edited together into a single
load module.)  Other than with this directive on, after a CANCEL it is
*required* that any program that has been cancelled MUST be entered in
initial state and not last-used state.  I know that Micro Focus (and FIPS)
tests this behavior - with EXIT PROGRAM - so I am very surprised by your
report.

Bill Wood wrote in message ...
>We are using MicroFocus NetExpress V2.0 and have found that if we use an
>EXIT PROGRAM to exit the program and then do a CANCEL after returning to
the
>calling program, the program is not actually cancelled.  When you call the
>program the next time, all of the variables are in the state they were when
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…

>>>unneccessary for the programmer to know in which way a specific module
```

```
On Fri, 11 Dec 1998 15:56:11, "Leif Svalgaard" <leif@ibm.net> wrote:

> Bill Wood wrote in message ...
> >We are using MicroFocus NetExpress V2.0 and have found that if we use an
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> this?

The present standard does not guarentee that a "CANCEL" will unload 
the program, but it is SUPPOSED to cause the next call to have WS 
values in their initial state.  But that MEANS only those items with a
VALUE clause.  This is the same as using "IS INITIAL" after the 
program ID.  However, if you manipulate Working-Storage items and the 
do not have a VALUE clause, they will be in the same state as they 
last were.... maybe.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: GoBack (was Re: Ersatz EXITs

_(reply depth: 28)_

- **From:** Don Nelson <don.nelson@compaq.com>
- **Date:** 1998-12-11T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<36716754.5D34@compaq.com>`
- **References:** `<3663DE5B.705D@swbell.net> <19981201231110.26237.00000075@ng101.aol.com> <#f3I6BeH#GA.142@upnetnews05> <743sa2$n28@sjx-ixn9.ix.netcom.com> <#1mOtUvH#GA.161@upnetnews05> <OkUdOVxI#GA.293@upnetnews03> <3671409c.0@news1.ibm.net> <Jl0PnHJ5PvPd-pn2-xEdFTLRdYddB@Dwight_Miller.iix.com> <367153a8.0@news1.ibm.net>`

```
Leif Svalgaard wrote:
> 
> Yes, that is how the CANCEL is supposed work.
> My question was about the difference between GOBACK
> and EXIT PROGRAM as far as CANCEL is concerned.

There is no difference whatsoever.  GOBACK is defined as an EXIT 
PROGRAM followed by a STOP RUN.  In a called program you get EXIT 
PROGRAM.  In the main program you get STOP RUN.  There are no other 
magical parts as there are in some current implementations.

By the way, I would hope that no one counts on stuff without VALUE 
clauses being in its last-used state after a CANCEL.  This could be a 
disaster.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: GoBack (was Re: Ersatz EXITs

_(reply depth: 24)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 1998-12-02T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7451l4$bda@dfw-ixnews5.ix.netcom.com>`
- **References:** `<3663DE5B.705D@swbell.net> <19981201231110.26237.00000075@ng101.aol.com> <#f3I6BeH#GA.142@upnetnews05> <743sa2$n28@sjx-ixn9.ix.netcom.com> <3665FDE7.503D@sprynet.com>`

```
EXIT PROGRAM has been part of the COBOL Standard since Inter-Program
Communication (e.g. CALL) was first introduced.

Frank Swarbrick wrote in message <3665FDE7.503D@sprynet.com>...
>William M. Klein wrote:
>>
>> GoBack was originally an IBM mainframe extension.  It does an EXIT
PROGRAM
>> in a subroutine but a Stop Run in a "main" routine (thus making it
>> unneccessary for the programmer to know in which way a specific module
will
>> be used). This feature is included in the draft of the next COBOL
Standard
>> (and is implemented as an extension in many existing mainframe and PC
>> compilers).
>>
>> FYI,
>>   Some compilers allow the following syntax (which is illegal according
to
>> the current Standard)
>>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>> The current Standard does NOT allow an "Exit Program" to be followed by
ANY
>> other imperative statement.  However, "logically" this is what a GoBack
does
>> (because "Exit Program" is a no-op in a main program).
>
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>work: frank.swarbrick@1stbank.com
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EXIT PARAGRAPH, PERFORM...THRU etc. (was: First COBOL compiler)

_(reply depth: 20)_

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc123@bellsouth.net>
- **Date:** 1998-12-02T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<tk592.16674$Gy.6991377@news4.mia>`
- **References:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk> <19981119102634.09602.00000036@ng-cr1.aol.com> <Tpc52.297$Sf1.435979@news3.mia> <3655866a.0@news3.uswest.net> <lSl52.587$Gy.710084@news4.mia> <3655f11c.0@news1.ibm.net> <OmB52.1091$Sf1.1090000@news3.mia> <3656ed2c.0@news1.ibm.net> <36579F79.329A@swbell.net> <36586f1b.0@news1.ibm.net> <73a5f6$643@dfw-ixnews6.ix.netcom.com> <365E1297.6359D1AF@ibm.net> <365EB29F.12DD@swbell.net> <3662BBC5.15280394@home.com> <36634331.6EDD@swbell.net> <qKQ82.16090$Gy.6464295@news4.mia> <3663DE5B.705D@swbell.net>`

```
Michael C. Kasten wrote:
>Judson McClendon wrote:
>>
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
>the GO TO is a trivial EXIT paragraph which does nothing substantive.

From a strictly narrow theoretical sense, perhaps.  But that is
stretching the point a bit, don't you think?  In practice, nobody is
going to be examining a single paragraph entirely absent from any
program context, particularly a PERFORM/THRU paragraph without the
matching exit. :-)

>Any use of PERFORM THRU, by definition, involves fall-through logic,
>even if it is of a trivial sort.  Whether such a style is desirable
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>by a code checker or by your eyeballs.

But when the style is specifically to code all paragraphs such that
every paragraph has exactly one EXIT paragraph, there is no real
'fall through' logic, because the EXIT paragraph is exactly that,
an exit point.  It is precisely as understandable as a paragraph
with no EXIT, because the paragraph and exit are one entity.  When
a programmer sees:

PROCEDURE DIVISION.

000000-CONTROL.
    PERFORM 000100-INITIALIZE
       THRU 000100-EXIT.
    PERFORM 000200-PROCESS
       THRU 000200-EXIT
        UNTIL (WS-END-FLAG = 1).
    PERFORM 000300-TERMINATE
       THRU 000300-EXIT.
000000-EXIT.
    STOP RUN.

000100-INITIALIZE.
    ...
000100-EXIT.
    EXIT.

000200-PROCESS.
    ...
000200-EXIT.
    EXIT.

000300-TERMINATE.
    ...
000300-EXIT.
    EXIT.

And every single paragraph in the program has the same pattern: a
paragraph always with one exact same numbered -EXIT, there is no
ambiguity.  Considerations of whether it is a good practice for
other reasons aside, even someone totally unfamiliar with the
practice would recognize that pattern in a program after only a
few seconds inspection.  Just the fact that every single PERFORM
was matched with a THRU -EXIT would tell anyone with even an ounce
of analytical ability that there were no GO TO's outside the
paragraph/exit. :-)
```

```
Judson McClendon wrote:
> 
> Michael C. Kasten wrote:

[snip -- quoting me quoting Judson quoting me, etc.]

> >Any use of PERFORM THRU, by definition, involves fall-through logic,
> >even if it is of a trivial sort.  Whether such a style is desirable
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> a programmer sees:

[snip -- code sample]

> And every single paragraph in the program has the same pattern: a
> paragraph always with one exact same numbered -EXIT, there is no
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> few seconds inspection.  

A few seconds inspection will be enough to recognize the pattern,
but not enough to verify that the pattern is consistently followed.

When I start working on an unfamiliar program, I don't dare start
adding or rearranging paragraphs until I know where all the
fall-through logic is.  That means I have to examine every single
GO TO and every single PERFORM THRU.  Even when they all fit a 
standardized pattern such as you describe, I don't know that they
do until I have verified it.  Typically this analysis is tedious
but not difficult, and I find nothing out of the ordinary, but it 
still takes time -- an amount of time roughly proportional to the 
number of GO TOs and PERFORM THRUs.

I really do perform this kind of analysis when faced with an 
unfamiliar program, and it's no fun, and I could be more productive
spending my time on something else.  However, it takes only a few
seconds if there are no GO TOs and no PERFORM THRUs -- all I need
is a couple of find commands in the editor, and usually no more than
a glance at the first paragraph to make sure it exits before falling
through into the rest of the program.

If you routinely use a code checker to enforce coding standards on
your own code, and you never have to muck with code left over from 
other people, then these considerations may be moot.  In that case
you are coding in a restricted subset of COBOL which excludes the
messier possibilities.

I am not so fortunate.  The messier possibilities are really possible, 
and sometimes occur, and I have to be sure I recognize them when they
do occur.

Michael C. Kasten	mck9@swbell.net
http://home.swbell.net/mck9/cobol/cobol.html
```

```
>But when the style is specifically to code all paragraphs such that

…[3 more quoted lines elided]…

That is precisely why I stopped doing it.  If you follow the standard
exactly, then it is completely redundant.  The only reason that you
really need it is as a mental reminder, and in case you want to
break the rule and do an emergency exit from the routine. As has
been noted, a PERFORM EMERGENCY-STOP does work, and
```

```
Donald Tees wrote:
>
>Judson McClendon wrote:
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>is a perfectly valid way of doing it.

We're beginning to circle back over old territory, but it depends on
the kind of programs you are writing.  In the kinds of programs I am
contracted write, there are very frequent situations when having a
PARAGRAPH EXIT (GO TO EXIT) is extremely useful.  I tried for almost
two years to code completely without it, but the code was simply more
complex and less clear.  In this situation, having an exit is very
much *not* redundant.  I never, never, never GO TO outside a paragraph
under any circumstances.  I PERFORM abort code.  My code checker would
flag a GO TO outside a paragraph in any case, so I don't have to worry
about unintentional ones.
```

```
Judson McClendon wrote in message ...

>the kind of programs you are writing.  In the kinds of programs I am
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…

Judson, you are so caught up in your style that you do not recognize
the xxxx-EXIT as another paragraph! You simply cannot avoid GO TO'ing
out of a paragraph, because that is what any GO TO does: it GOes
out of the current paragraph and TO another one.

paragraph, which they then become in your mind (which is good).
```

```
>Judson McClendon wrote
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
>paragraph, which they then become in your mind (which is good).

Well, the GOTO could be to the top of the paragraph. ;-)  But yes, used
in this way they are one inseparable construct.  I agree with you, of
course, that technically it is two paragraphs.  But is not the goal of
abstraction to mentally think of and deal with a complex of entities as
one conceptual entity?  I have merely abstracted the paragraph/exit as
one entity. :-)
```

```

Leif Svalgaard wrote in message <3665b475.0@news1.ibm.net>...

>gee, I didn't think of that, maybe because I have never
>in 30 years done that...


A goto to the paragraph name is the *only* goto I have
coded in at least the last 20 years of Cobol. I have used
that, though.  Typical:

READ-FILE-WITH-FILE-LOCK.
       OPEN I-O FILENAME.
       IF FILENAME-STATUS IS EQUAL TO "35"
              PERFORM CREATE-NEW-FILE
       ELSE
       IF FILENAME-STATUS IS NOT EQUAL TO "00"
              do a display and a dispatch
              GOTO READ-FILE-WITH-FILE-LOCK.

I have stopped doing that as well, though.  I now code it
as a perform until status="00".  The reason is that I got
caught on it when I cut and pasted a paragraph, and
forgot to change the goto. Took me a week to figure out
what was going on, as the file was never opened for more
than a fractional second.  One time in a thousand it would
go off into the wild blue yonder, and once outside that
paragraph boundary, you are well and truly screwed.

Now I *never* use a goto.
```

```
In a similar manner:

*
     PERFORM R600-GET-INFILE-REC THRU R600-GIR-EX
      UNTIL NO-MORE-INPUT OR VALID-INREC-FOUND.
     IF VALID-INREC-FOUND
         quack quack quack
     ELSE
     IF NO-MORE-INPUT
         woof woof woof.
*     
 R600-GET-INFILE-REC.
*
     READ INFILE INTO WS-INFILE-REC
         AT END SET NO-MORE-INPUT TO TRUE
                GO TO R600-GIR-EX.
*
     ADD +1 TO INFILE-RECS-READ-CTR
     IF INREC-FAILS-COND00
         ADD +1 TO RECS-FAILED-COND00-CTR
         GO TO R600-GET-INFILE-REC.
*
     IF INREC-FAILS-COND01
         ADD +1 TO RECS-FAILED-COND01-CTR
         GO TO R600-GET-INFILE-REC.
...

     IF INREC-FAILS-CONDFF
         ADD +1 TO RECS-FAILED-CONDFF-CTR
         GO TO R600-GET-INFILE-REC.
*
     SET VALID-INREC-FOUND TO TRUE.
*
 R600-GIR-EX.  EXIT.

(yow... how's *that* for a rather 'unaesthetic' mixture of New and Olde
Styles?)

A proof of the undeniable efficiency of this method is left to someone
else.

DD
```

- **From:** Howard Brazee <NOSPAMbrazee@home.com>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3666F460.FC14694A@home.com>`

```
If you have a working standard using go to exit, fine.  But if you are
writing standards, I recommend disallowing these.  They might not be
sufficiently better enough to replace your existing standards, but they
are enough better to put them in as your first set of standards.

And believe it or not, lots of places don't follow coding standards.
```

```
Interestingly, that saying "the exeption proves the rule" derived from a Latin
phrase that uses a word that *sounds* like prove but more accurately translates
to "the exception tests the rule". The original meaning was replaced by a
botched translation.
```

```
Judson McClendon wrote in message ...
>Michael C. Kasten wrote:
>>Judson McClendon wrote:
…[82 more quoted lines elided]…
>paragraph/exit. :-)


I can attest to the efficacy of Judson's PERFORM THRU methodology.  I was in
on the ground floor (as a designer, coder and consultant) in the development
of a large-scale, complex back-office processing system sold to the
securities brokerage industry for use in-house or as an on-line
service-bureau basis.  Coding for this system (virtually entirely COBOL)
began in 1982 and it went into live production early in 1983.  All
programming used the PERFORM THRU technique, just as it is described by
Judson, 'religiously' (the programming standards manual was/is even called
the 'COBOL Bible'.)  This system has been tremendously expanded, enhanced
and maintained under those same rules ever since, and it is now ranked with
the leaders among the relatively few comprehensive, non-proprietary
back-office data processing systems available to the securities industry.
```

```

Donald Tees wrote in message <743ijc$j2o$1@news.igs.net>...
>Dennis J. Minette wrote in message ...
>
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>

You are absolutely correct on your first observation - it _was_ the rigorous
adherence to the in-house standard that brought about the success of the
original development and the subsequent enhancemant and maintenance cycles.
All the founders and original participants in the endeavor could not reach
unanimity on using top-down, structured code, with no GOTOs allowed, so the
agreed upon compromise was, more or less:
a.) code _all_ PERFORMs as a PERFORM THRU construct (even the most trivial
ones),
b.) code _every_ THRU paragraph to use the name of the
performed-paragraph-name with a -EXIT appended at the end,
c.) allow coding of _only_ these kind of _matched  pair_ paragraphs (with no
intervening paragraphs to be inserted between them),
d.) try _real hard_ (somewhat more than a good faith effort) to NOT use any
GO TO statements

On top of this (but not a mandatory, required part of the COBOL coding
bible), I coded the _matched pairs_ of paragraph names thusly:

    PERFORM functionally-named-routine
             THRU functionally-named-routine-EXIT

This made it very easy to scan a program listing, or scroll it by on the
screen, and see immediately where a mismatched paragraph-name _pair_ had
been perpetrated.  But the compiler found such mismatches even easier than
any human eyeballs could!

Dennis Minette
CCCO
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EXIT PARAGRAPH, PERFORM...THRU etc. (was: First COBOL compiler)

_(reply depth: 24)_

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc123@bellsouth.net>
- **Date:** 1998-12-06T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Sywa2.7302$gl.2547397@news2.mia>`
- **References:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk> <19981119102634.09602.00000036@ng-cr1.aol.com> <Tpc52.297$Sf1.435979@news3.mia> <3655866a.0@news3.uswest.net> <lSl52.587$Gy.710084@news4.mia> <3655f11c.0@news1.ibm.net> <OmB52.1091$Sf1.1090000@news3.mia> <3656ed2c.0@news1.ibm.net> <36579F79.329A@swbell.net> <36586f1b.0@news1.ibm.net> <73a5f6$643@dfw-ixnews6.ix.netcom.com> <365E1297.6359D1AF@ibm.net> <365EB29F.12DD@swbell.net> <3662BBC5.15280394@home.com> <36634331.6EDD@swbell.net> <qKQ82.16090$Gy.6464295@news4.mia> <3663DE5B.705D@swbell.net> <tk592.16674$Gy.6991377@news4.mia> <OCBr#ReH#GA.220@upnetnews05> <743ijc$j2o$1@news.igs.net> <eJ5FEljH#GA.298@upnetnews05>`

```
Dennis J. Minette wrote:
>
>On top of this (but not a mandatory, required part of the COBOL coding
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>

That is exactly how I first coded using PERFORM/THRU, except that I
always used paragraph numbers.

     PERFORM 012300-functionally-named-routine
        THRU 012300-functionally-named-routine-EXIT

But with the code checker, programmers do not have to visually spot
the mismatch, so I shortened the EXIT to:

     PERFORM 012300-functionally-named-routine
        THRU 012300-EXIT

Where the paragraph numbers are always the same.  The checker simply
flags duplicate paragraph numbers, enforces that EXIT paragraphs have
the same number as the paragraph, that a PERFORM/THRU pair has the
same number, and that any GO TO in a paragraph has the same number as
the paragraph.  Using the abbreviated EXIT would be dangerous without
the checker, but it takes much less typing, and is perfectly safe
with the checker.

I'm not advocating that everybody use paragraph numbers.  I don't use
them in other languages.  But I have found them useful in COBOL for
several reasons.  First, COBOL programs are often very large, and in
online programs there can be many sections of code which are very
similar, but not identical or close enough to reuse common routines.
For example, the overall logic in processing an update screen is the
same most of the time.  So in each one you have routines to validate
the fields, move the data to the database record, etc.  At least in
my code, these paragraphs are named the same for consistency.  But
since the fields vary between screens, you can't use the same code.
So I use the first two digits of the 6 digit paragraph name to
identify the screen.  Each online program is menu driven, and the
menu items are selected with a 2 digit number.  That same 2 digit
number is used in the Working Storage for the screen data areas:

   03  RECV-03-NAME         PIC  X(30).
   03  RECV-03-ADDRESS      PIC  X(30).
    ...
   03  SEND-03-NAME         PIC  X(30).
   03  SEND-03-ADDRESS      PIC  X(30).

and in every paragraph of the code which processes that screen:

   030000-CUSTOMER-UPDATE.
    ...
   031000-VALIDATE-FIELDS.

The mainframe systems I work on use transaction codes to identify
which program is to process the screen.  The transaction codes also
use that same number.  An alphanumeric code could be used, but the
number can be used directly as a subscript, which can be very useful.
In any other approach I have seen, I would lose one or more of the
small advantages this gives.  This is where I would disagree with
those who say that it is the strict discipline and consistency, not
the particular methods, which create the result.  Yes, the strict
discipline and consistency are very important.  But the particular
methods *do* matter.  I have spent some 25 years seriously studying
this issue, and I believe that when you carefully choose your coding
style, each little choice can make a small bit of difference.  The
small bit of difference may not be significant, but the cumulative
effect of many of them can be very significant.  Not that there is
only one 'best way', or even that there is a truly 'best way'.  But
the particular choices you make in developing your coding style can
be more significant than you may realize.

I get the impression that many programmers have made choices from
what they have been taught, or from theory (e.g. GO TO is bad),
rather than from actually trying different methods.  Theory is fine,
but there is nothing like actually trying something for a while to
give you a sense for how it really works.  We old dogs are so set
in our ways that we're unlikely to change. :)  But I would really
recommend to you younger programmers that you spend some time
actually experimenting with different techniques.  And don't go for
what is P.C. or for what just looks good.  Go for what works well.
What is pleasing to the eye is not always what the eye assimilates
best, because what is pleasing to view can be more emotional than
functional.
```

```
Well, all good arguments get re-hashed every so often... and this argument
does, too.  Mr McClendon, I disagree with you on One Small Point; consider
the following:

 PROCEDURE DIVISION.
*
     PERFORM A100-HOUSEKEEPING THRU A100-EX.
     PERFORM M500-MAINLINE     THRU M500-EX
      UNTIL CONDITION-TO-END-PROCESSING.
     PERFORM Z900-EOJ          THRU Z900-EX.
     GOBACK.
*
 A100-HOUSEKEEPING.
*
     OPEN INPUT INFILE.
     IF NOT GOOD-INFILE-IO
         MOVE ' A100-HOUSEKEEPING '  TO WS-ABEND-PARA
         MOVE ' OPEN ERROR        '  TO WS-ABEND-REASON
         MOVE ' INFILE            '  TO WS-ABEND FILNAM
         MOVE INFILSTAT              TO WS-ABEND-FILSTAT
         GO TO Z999-ALL-FALL-DOWN.

...

 Z999-ALL-FALL-DOWN.
*
     ACCEPT WS-ABEND-DATE   FROM DATE.
     ACCEPT WS-ABEND-TIME   FROM TIME.
     CALL ABEND-LOGGING-PGM USING WS-ABEND-AREA.
     DISPLAY ' ', WS-ABEND-AREA-LINE01.
     DISPLAY ' ', WS-ABEND-AREA-LINE02.
...
     GOBACK.

... and notice two things:

1) It is, one should hope, *eminently* readable code (not *strictly*
Y2K-compliant but Life is Tough); if one of the less-senior readers here
would like to point out ambiguities and/or difficulties then I would
appreciated being made aware of them.

2) It has not one but *two* 'firing offenses':  The GO TO outside a
paragraph's label or -EXIT paragraph *and* a second point of program exit
as well... two GOBACKs.

Yes, Mr McClendon, in general I would agree with your arguments BUT... I
find that a structure such as this reinforces the rule *precisely* because
it breaks it.

DD
```

```
On Tue, 1 Dec 1998 18:26:22, Howard Brazee <NOSPAMbrazee@home.com> 
wrote:

> I agree that it makes sense to GO TO ABORT-ROUTINE.  Some people PERFORM
> ABORT-ROUTINE, but this is a silly fiction which everybody knows is a
> lie.
> 

I'm guilty of that --- but here' s my reasoning.

Sometimes you just HAVE to ABEND.  I PERFORM the ABEND routine.  Why? 
If I GO TO it, it defeats the optimization (the compiler WILL NOT 
optimize the program) and I lose runtime speed.  Using perform 
accomplishes two things - I get optimization (CALL directive with 
Realia), and I get rid of the GO TO <G>.
```

```
On Wed, 2 Dec 1998 15:20:20 -0500, "Dennis J. Minette"
<dennis_minette@email.msn.com> wrote:

>
>Thane Hubbell wrote in message ...
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
>

From page 11-13 of the Realia 4.2 manual: (any typos are mine)

PERFORM CALL directive.

The compiler uses one of two mechanisms to PERFORM a procedure in
COBOL.  The first is fully compatible with the ANSI standard.  It
permits you to invoke a set of procedure code via PERFORM and GO TO
verbs and "fallthrough" from above, all in the same program.  This
method is the default and is in effect when the NOCALL directive is
present.  (See chapter 3, Compiling a Program).  A faster method is
available, if your program satisfies certain additional criteria:

  A PERFORM range is never invoked by any ohter meaans than a PERFORM
of that range.  GO TOS and fallthourhgs into the range never occur.

  PERFORM ranges never overlap: an exit point is always an exit point
(never fallthrough).

  A PERFORM range is never terminated by a GO TO out of the range;
that is a GO TO that never returns.  The PERFORM range terminates only
by falling through the last or only paragraph in the range.

Structured programs usually meet these requirements.  For such
programs, specify the CALL directive.  When the CALL directive is
active, the compiler can use the hardware stack to hold return
addresses.  Otherwise, special return cells must be allocated in the
data segment.  Accessin gthe stack is much faster.  To execute a
simple PERFORM with the CALL directive takes 27 clock cycles on an IBM
PC; with NOCALL, 72 clock cycles.  This can have significant execution
impace on the execution of an inner loop.  CA-Realia COBOL can perform
extensive code optimizations when this directive is n effect.

--------------------
Thane again.  What they do not tell you is that is also greatly
reduces the size of the code segment.  Sorry it took so long to answer
this - the news server at work is still crippled and I did not see
this message from there.
```

```
On 2 Dec 1998 15:36:49 GMT, docdwarf@clark.net () enlightened us:

>In article <sI592.16684$Gy.6999540@news4.mia>,
>Judson McClendon <judmc123@bellsouth.net> wrote:
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
>DD

I'm amazed at the length of this thread!  We are really discussing a
style issue more than anything else here.  Our shop standards are
exactly what Judson has been describing.  All of our paragraphs are
perform thru an exit and the only go  to is to an exit paragraph.  All
6,000 or so programmers employed by our company are quite happy and
comfortable with this construct and it has been this way for at least
10 years.  I really see nothing wrong with this construct.  I am
really dismayed at those who refuse to or hate to use GO TO.  Yes, it
can be abused, but not to use it at all is a wonderment.  Why should
Cobol be different in its use of a branch instruction than any other
language?  Can you imagine coding Assembler without being able to use
a branch insturction?  Or Basic without a Jump or Branch instruction?
I suppose there will be those who say those languages don't lend
themselves to be structered the way Cobol does, but, again, isn't it
just a matter of discipline and style?  As long as one is consistent
and the programs are readable, efficient and maintainable, what does
it matter?

Just my .02 cents worth.

Regards,

          ////
         (o o)
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EXIT PARAGRAPH, PERFORM...THRU etc. (was: First COBOL compiler)

_(reply depth: 24)_

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc123@bellsouth.net>
- **Date:** 1998-12-03T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<g2A92.776$Jl.207081@news3.mia>`
- **References:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk> <sI592.16684$Gy.6999540@news4.mia> <743mqh$bpj$1@callisto.clark.net> <07DD703D74B51C4E.DBF29CF2086E0891.C254998AAE8F04EF@library-proxy.airnews.net> <746b92$49g$1@clarknet.clark.net>`

```
docdwarf@clark.net wrote:
>
>AAAAAIIIIIEEEEEEEEE!!!  'But we've *always* done it this way and
>*thousands* of folks think Everything Is Just Fine so if you don't like
>it... [snip]

I can understand that reaction. :-)  But in fact, a history of doing
things a certain way does have some significance and merit.  If a
certain method or practice has been proven successful, you should only
change direction when you *know* you are going to get a return, and
that the return will be sufficient to justify the effort of converting
to the new method or practice.  Abandoning a known and satisfactory
situation for an unsure one is foolhardy.  I believe our profession has
demonstrated a poor regard for that consideration in the past.  We are
so fascinated with the 'nouveau technicalis' ;) that we, particularly
academics and younger programmers, forget that technology is only as
valuable as we actually receive benefit from it.  I am sure many of you
can remember decision tables.  At the time the PC line was that if you
weren't using decision tables you were a dinosaur.  Not to get another
war started, but I see much the same mindset with OO.  Time will tell
if I am correct or not.  But like buying into anything, we should take
a look at proven benefits, not 'pie in the sky'.  Make sure it will be
a real benefit before jumping on the wagon.

Consider my situation vis-�-vis these discussions on PERFORM/EXIT as an
example.  If I were to abandon my own tried and true coding standards
I would still have to deal with millions of lines of code written by
the old standard, and I would be constantly switching back and forth
for many years (and I don't have *that* many left :).  Unless all my
clients also changed their coding standards to match, I would still
need to code by the old standard for them indefinitely.  Even if there
is real benefit to the change, it would have to be very significant to
warrant changing my own practices.  To me, that is simply common sense looking at the bottom line: What does it cost and what is the
payback?
Other programmers (or myself) looking at the same decision from a
different situation could reasonably come to a different conclusion.
```

```
On 3 Dec 1998 17:55:36 GMT, docdwarf@clark.net () enlightened us:

>In article <g2A92.776$Jl.207081@news3.mia>,
>Judson McClendon <judmc123@bellsouth.net> wrote:
…[33 more quoted lines elided]…
>DD

I wasn't implying that "its always been done that way, so it must be
right".  Obviously that is erroneous thinking.  What I was questioning
or, more correctly, more amazed about was the discussions going on
about Perform Thru and to use or not to use a Go To.  

I think Judson has pointed out the pitfalls of changing styles
mid-stream.  The question remains, what are, if any, real benefits of
using no Perform Thru and/or Go To.  It sort of reminds me of
discussions in this NG in the past about other languages vs. Cobol
being better or not.  It always seems that the latest language, e.g. C
or C++, is described by its proponents as being better than Cobol
because of this or that reason.  Then we Cobol programmers get our
hackles up and defend Cobol tooth and nail.  Soon the discussion dies
out.  So the latest vogue in coding Cobol is to not use Perform Thru
and Go To.  And we'll discuss it to no end with no real resolution.
As with the discussion of Cobol vs. other languages, the real test is
the test of time.  Will Perform Thru and Go To disappear to be
replaced by some other construct?  Will Cobol give way to Java or some
as yet unpublished language?  Only time will tell.

Regards,

          ////
         (o o)
-oOO--(_)--OOo-
Microsoft Windows:  Proof that P.T. Barnum was correct.
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

- **References:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk> <qKQ82.16090$Gy.6464295@news4.mia> <7411lh$koj$1@callisto.clark.net> <sI592.16684$Gy.6999540@news4.mia> <743mqh$bpj$1@callisto.clark.net> <07DD703D74B51C4E.DBF29CF2086E0891.C254998AAE8F04EF@library-proxy.airnews.net>`

```
SkippyPB wrote:
> 
(snip)
> Why should Cobol be different in its use of a branch instruction than any other
> language?  Can you imagine coding Assembler without being able to use
> a branch insturction?

No, but that's not the issue here. Trying to think of a good analogy
here and I've come up with the logical view of a database vs. the
physical implementation. The idea behind structured programming is that
one codes the program using only certain constructs, Branch/Jump/GOTO
not being among them, and the structure applies only to the source. What
happens when it's made binary is not an issue and does not relate to
structured programming. Do you apply your same reasoning to the ALTER
verb, as well? After all, it's there, why not use it? BTW, if you code
Assembler using the structured macros you don't code branches, either.

Hoping this sheds some light,
Bill Lynch
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3662BAEB.F6D291E6@home.com>`
- **References:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk> <19981119102634.09602.00000036@ng-cr1.aol.com> <Tpc52.297$Sf1.435979@news3.mia> <3655866a.0@news3.uswest.net> <lSl52.587$Gy.710084@news4.mia> <3655f11c.0@news1.ibm.net> <OmB52.1091$Sf1.1090000@news3.mia> <3656ed2c.0@news1.ibm.net> <36579F79.329A@swbell.net> <36586f1b.0@news1.ibm.net> <73a5f6$643@dfw-ixnews6.ix.netcom.com> <365E1297.6359D1AF@ibm.net>`

```
> A. Perform only single paragraphs. Do not use GO TO or sections.

...

> The advocates of Style A are GO-TO-less programmers in the tradition of
> Dikstra and Hoare. The result is an easy-to-follow subroutine structure,
> but (IMHO) often awkward structures within subroutines. The
> EXIT-procedure statement will help, but an EXIT-structure statement
> would be better.

Or sometimes we are programmers who have seen maintenance done where
maintenance programmers put paragraphs places where they get performed
accidentally.  It isn't GO TO which bothers me, it is dropping into
paragraphs accidentally which causes trouble.  So I like to avoid
dropping into paragraphs at all.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EXIT PARAGRAPH, PERFORM...THRU etc. (was: First COBOL compiler)

_(reply depth: 12)_

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc123@bellsouth.net>
- **Date:** 1998-11-22T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<MCU52.1521$Gy.1550184@news4.mia>`
- **References:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk> <19981119102634.09602.00000036@ng-cr1.aol.com> <Tpc52.297$Sf1.435979@news3.mia> <3655866a.0@news3.uswest.net> <lSl52.587$Gy.710084@news4.mia> <3655f11c.0@news1.ibm.net> <OmB52.1091$Sf1.1090000@news3.mia> <3656ed2c.0@news1.ibm.net> <36579F79.329A@swbell.net>`

```
That's a pretty good explanation of my position, Michael, probably
better than I could have written. :-)  The only points I would add
to your comments is that I only use the code generator when writing
new code, not for modifications, and that the generator is pretty
simple, only generating about 6-8 lines of code per data name.  The
code is exactly what I would write if I were hand coding it.  It is
such a frequent and consistent pattern that generation is easy.

I admit that if I did not encounter this particular pattern of code
(the many sequential simple error tests) so very often, and if my
clients used COBOL 85 instead of COBOL 74 (no END-xxx constructs),
I would seriously consider dropping GO TO EXIT now, even before EXIT
PARAGRAPH is available.  And those long series of simple error tests
are very frequently interspersed with longer complex conditionals
which test various error conditions between fields.  Coding those in
the way suggested by Michael and others without END-xxx would be
pretty messy.  Those could be made into separate paragraphs at the
expense of extra code, and I often do that.  But it is not always
logically clearer to do it that way.  I really do prefer to avoid
GO TO. :-)
```

```
Leif Svalgaard wrote:
>
>I would argue that one should not even exit after one error. How would
>you like it if the friendly Cobol compiler only reported the first error
>it found? A better way would be to highlight each field that is in error
>and thus necessiate checking all of them, hence no GO TO exit.

That is because we give an actual error message at the bottom of the
screen, and there is only room for one error message.  We also highlight
the field(s) which cause error.  Understand that the error checking in
some of these systems is very complex.  One field might be in error from
a dozen different possible causes.  Merely highlighting the fields with
no, or insufficient, error message text would be unacceptable.
```

```
Judson McClendon wrote in message ...
>Leif Svalgaard wrote:
>>
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>no, or insufficient, error message text would be unacceptable.


I understand that, so you would, of course, always show a message.
If you check from the bottom up, the message that the user sees will
pertain to the first field in error (there was a thread about this a while
back), but should there be other fields with errors these will be
highlighted too. Users are not stupid and will appreciate the subtle hint
about the other errors.

I actually once had a *requirement* from a customer to highlight
ALL errors. I did have to point out that I could only find *most* errors
because some errors could mask others if you have complex
logic. Anyway, checking all that you can is good practice.
Since errors are rare, checking for all of them is not an efficiency
issue.
```

```
First, I am very concerned about maintenance efficiency, hardly
concerned about run efficiency.  My attention is towards the most costly
element.

I don't like having paragraphs with umpteen lines in them.  If there are
logical choices to make, I prefer to do performs instead of rechecking
switches over and over again.  I try to make a paragraph do one thing
only.  Sure it may make the optimizer not work as well, but see my first
paragraph!
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EXIT PARAGRAPH, PERFORM...THRU etc. (was: First COBOL compiler)

_(reply depth: 11)_

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@aiservices.com>
- **Date:** 1998-11-22T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6KV52.112$7a.146@news1.atlantic.net>`
- **References:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk> <19981119102634.09602.00000036@ng-cr1.aol.com> <Tpc52.297$Sf1.435979@news3.mia> <3655866a.0@news3.uswest.net> <lSl52.587$Gy.710084@news4.mia> <3655f11c.0@news1.ibm.net> <OmB52.1091$Sf1.1090000@news3.mia> <3656ed2c.0@news1.ibm.net>`

```

Leif Svalgaard wrote in message <3656ed2c.0@news1.ibm.net>...
>
>I think that having *a* style is better than having *no* style. Consistency
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>
I hope that you understand that GO TO xxxx-EXIT is not separable from
PERFORM paragraph-name THRU paragraph-exit-name. It is the
pairing of these two, along with some rules, that creates the "style."

From the book, Modular Programming in COBOL by Russell M.
Armstrong, 1973, page 77.

" 1. GO TO should refer only to a name within its own paragraph-
group. ...

" 2. GO TO should branch only forward in the program (downward
on the page), and (by rule 1) only to an intermediate paragraph or
to the EXIT paragraph within the paragraph-group. ...

" 3. GO TO should branch forward only to the EXIT paragraph in
its paragraph-group.

" The most desireable degree of GO TO control is achieved by
adhering as closely as possible to rule 3. This is not always
feasible, but because of habit rather than for technical reasons.
GO TO has always provided an easy way out of structural
difficulties, so much so that programmers have been able to
avoid designing their programs. As an individual gains
experience in developing modularity and consciously seeks
to reduce the untamed use of GO TO, this style of programming
will become more natural; it takes practice to use new tools.
The reward will be modules that are more readable, easier
to document, and eventually faster and easier to program and
verify (a term I prefer to the pesticidal "debug").

" The two main tools for achieving COBOL program modularity
thus are: (1) separation of the code into PERFORMed paragraphs,
and (2) preservation of their separateness by the restriction of the
of the use of GO TO."
-------------------------------
Rick Smith
e-mail: < ricksmith@aiservices.com >
```

```
> Chris Osborne wrote:
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> general public? 

[snip]

So some of us don't like your GO TOs -- big deal.  That doesn't mean
we couldn't take some inspiration from your code generator, or your
code checker, or the thought processes that lead you to the very
conclusions that we may still disagree with.  It's the thinking that
matters, not the petty preferences of style.  I'd take a well-placed
GO TO any day over an ill-considered jumble of formally structured
paragraphs.  I can always eliminate the GO TO if I don't like it, but
it's tougher to fix a bad design.

I wouldn't expect to agree with everything you say.  I don't always
agree with everything I say, either.

Michael C. Kasten	mck9@swbell.net
```
