FYI...
before you start cashing your royalty checks, be aware that many
programmers refuse to buy ANY book.  I've been writing for 8 years and
been in contact with some major mainframe (i.e., cobol)publishers and
the books don't sell as you might anticipate. yes, they do sell; yes
it is a nice piece of additional income, but it's amazing how many
programmers refuse to buy any book at all.  i recently coordinated an
effort with a publisher to allow a 45% discount with no tax and no
shipping costs for a team of 100+ programmers ---  only 4 bought
books, for a total of 10 books.   it's not a place to get rich.... but
my best wishes to your success....
david

> FYI...
> before you start cashing your royalty checks, be aware that many
> programmers refuse to buy ANY book. 

I wonder.  Lots of shops do not have sufficient documentation, and
programmers need some references.  The non-company book which I see the
most often in MVS shops is SYSTEM/370 JOB CONTROL LANGUAGE.  I think the
trick is to supply a book which is a great reference so that programmers
don't have to search down manuals to convert between EXAMINE, TRANSFORM,
and INSPECT.

The trouble is, what they want most is compiler dependent.  What to look
for when it isn't obvious what the error message is pointing to.
```

"Judson McClendon" <judmc123@bellsouth.net>
 Mon, 23 Nov 1998 20:43:17 GMT
wrote
 >> I realize you may be being facetious Chris, but I'm already working on a
book.  Two of my clients have asked me to document all this stuff in more
detail than I ever have, and agree to pay me for it.  I hope to turn it
into a book.  The (shall we say cool? :) reception some of my ideas have
received here make me wonder if such a book would be well received by the
general public? ;-)  No sour grapes, I'm serious.  If it is not clear to
you knowledgeable and experienced folks whether this stuff is any good
(though you have only seen small pieces of the whole), what good would a
book do?  I wouldn't be doing it for the money, but to 'pass along what I

<<

Knowledgeable, smoledgeable! You have frequently demonstrated a thoroughness
that is all too rare. And very few people are as knowledgeable as you about
COBOL.

You are definitely one of the best writers contributing to this newsgroup. You
have very good ideas and the experience to back up your views. You definitely
should publish and get some reward for your hard earned understandings.

If you do provide your clients with preliminary releases of your book, be sure
to get advice about how to structure any agreements (so that you retain all
rights for further publishing). Protecting your rights is not easy to
understand. Hope you do not enter into casual agreements (or atleast not enter
agreements casually).

You definitely should publish. But don't try to do it all at once. Brush up
what you have. Try to package that much (put a cover on it and a table of
contents).  Pick reasonable goals for yourself, like during the coming holiday
finish one additional important subject (not the whole book).  Keep picking the
next goal in a way that can just be put under the next wrapper (cover plus a
revised table of contents).

You konw a modern CPU is like that.  They send a simple mask to the foundary
and start stamping out chips (call them books). Next they add a few
instructions, and release the next version. Then they add the advanced math or
fast graphics, and out the door goes release 3.  

Think of your early releases as something that can do a lot of good just like
your consulting and personal advising. You are a generator. You are a source.
There is nothing wrong with you making a permanent recordation of your
inspiring style.

Publish.

Find meaningful break points at which to put wraps on it and publish. Then do
it again.

You are one of the good ones. Publish.

Best Wishes,

Robert Rayhawk
RKRayhawk@aol.com
```

Bob Wolfe wrote:
>
>...AND just to make you feel even better, it is an undisputed fact

>it!)

Sadly, I must admit that I have traded the 3000GT VR-4. :-(  I had no
choice.  It was either that or the state of Alabama was going to pull
my drivers license when I got another speeding ticket.  That car would
do 0-60 in under 5 seconds.  It was like driving a carnival ride!  Just
normally accelerating onto an Interstate, I would look down and be
doing 90-100.  The steering on that car was extremely tight, you twitch
the wheel, the car moves.  It was a driver's car, Motor Trend placed it
in the same performance class as the Ferrari Testarosa.  Driving this
kind of car is a constant rush.  It would go 60-100 in two or three
seconds.  Just passing another car was a kick, and the handling was
indescribable.  It took curves effortlessly at speeds you would never
think of in a regular car, as if it was on rails, and the brakes would
pop your eyes out.  It is impossible (for me anyway) to drive a car like
that without breaking the speed limit.  It's like riding a huge tiger,
straining at the leash, ready to leap at your slightest whim.  In my
younger days, I logged many miles in excess of 100 mph, but never over
about 135.  One night about 2 AM on a long straight stretch of empty
road I decided to see how fast the VR-4 would go.  As the needle climbed
past 155, the yellow lines became a solid blur, and the telephone poles
did indeed look like a picket fence.  I began to think about mortality
and what a person would look like spread over an acre of highway.  I
decided I didn't *really* need to know just how fast it would go.  But
for a while, it was really, really fun. :-)

Cost?  With every option available, $36k new in 1992.  A set tires was
over $1000.  Worth every penny.

For you single guys, women would follow me around in that thing. :-)

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EXIT PARAGRAPH, PERFORM...THRU etc. (was: First COBOL compiler)

_(reply depth: 10)_

- **From:** "Chris Osborne" <chris_n_osborne@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 1998-11-24T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<365ad5e6.0@news2.uswest.net>`
- **References:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk> <19981119102634.09602.00000036@ng-cr1.aol.com> <Tpc52.297$Sf1.435979@news3.mia> <3655866a.0@news3.uswest.net> <lSl52.587$Gy.710084@news4.mia> <3659bf9d.0@news2.uswest.net> <FNj62.2086$w_6.2122497@news2.mia>`

```
Judson,

I don't know how that you perceived that I was being facetious.  Honest, I
wasn't.


Personally, I'm over in the 'perform a paragraph without ever using gotos or
sections' group.  However, I am not stuck on that.  I find that discussions
about how structured programming or oop are just tools apply to programming
styles as well.  When you are a journalist, you write one way.    Sometimes,
writing style differs between newspapers, and often is based on an editor's
desires.  I think that programming styles often work the same way.

The number of highly efficient coding styles developed by individual
programmers is probably equal to the number of highly efficient programmers.
Lots.  This goes directly into such questions as shop-level or even
enterprise-level coding and style standards, are they a good or bad thing?
Does style influence system architecture?  Or does style eventually, in the
hands of the experienced, finally equate to automatic architecture (i.e.
your own style automatically defines certain sub-types of programming
architecture)?

I have no good answers, just ones that lead to more questions.  Questions
that bring the desire to 'do things your own way' face to face with 'do
things the same way'.  I'm not about to announce that one way is better than
another.  I'll just have to continue exploring the matter.


Whether I ever adopt your style is a different matter (as you said, your
client/employer often determines what style will be used), but I thought
reading about it would be a) fun and b) give me a look at something beyond
what I'm doing here.  Your data dictionary system sounds fascinating.


If you're already going to put something into print on a limited basis like
this, as long as the legal bits and pieces allow you to, you should propose
a commercial book to one of the major publishers.  The worst they can do is
say no.



Judson McClendon wrote in message ...
>Chris Osborne wrote:
>>It sounds like you clearly have the experience to write a book on this
>>subject.  Why don't you?  You could include evidence from the various
shops
>>that have adopted your methodology (for whatever reason) about how defect
>>rates go down and code writing rates go up.
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EXIT PARAGRAPH, PERFORM...THRU etc. (was: First COBOL compiler)

_(reply depth: 12)_

- **From:** "Chris Osborne" <chris_n_osborne@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 1998-11-25T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<365c3401.0@news3.uswest.net>`
- **References:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk> <19981119102634.09602.00000036@ng-cr1.aol.com> <Tpc52.297$Sf1.435979@news3.mia> <3655866a.0@news3.uswest.net> <lSl52.587$Gy.710084@news4.mia> <3659bf9d.0@news2.uswest.net> <FNj62.2086$w_6.2122497@news2.mia> <365ad5e6.0@news2.uswest.net> <365ADA85.755ACE84@home.com>`

```
Howard Brazee wrote in message <365ADA85.755ACE84@home.com>...
>> The number of highly efficient coding styles developed by individual
>> programmers is probably equal to the number of highly efficient
programmers.
>
>I think a primary need though is to define "highly efficient
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>programmer".

I will immediately agree with that last statement.


And here are my two cents.

Being "highly efficient" as a programmer combines elements of the following
(different people have more, or less, of these characteristics; these are
not ranked in order):

1-Typing Speed & Quality.
2-Determination/Tenacity.
3-Good organizational skills.
4-Good writing skills (for documentation, not code; avoiding spelling and
basic grammar errors doesn't matter nearly as much as communicating the why
behind the code; the code by itself usually tells you what).
5-Really liking working with computers doesn't hurt.
6-Patience.
7-Problem/Puzzle solving skills.
8-Language knowledge.
9-OS knowledge.


Well, I've written a bunch of characteristics, but I find they don't really
determine what is "highly efficient".  Maybe we could say that a "highly
efficient" programmer is someone who gets the job done to the satisfaction
of the user (or boss), on time, within budget, without ruining their life,
and who leaves behind a system (or portion of a system) that can be admired
by other programmers (this last comes directly from what Howard said in his
post).


All of this is a pretty narrow definition, though.  Other ideas?
```

Judson - 
  I think that what you are running into is a paradigm shift. <grin>
I *strongly* suggest working in a OO environment, using Java or 
Smalltalk or another OO language. Even a relatively small project,
such as a Purchase Order system or something. Don't use a hybrid
langauge such as C++ or Basic.

  It will paint a lot of things you already know in a very different
light, and I expect that you will at some point, click over and 
become an OO aherent, with fervor. :) 
 
  It would certianly make a difference in the way you would view things
like PERFORM THRU and functions.

-Paul

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: EXIT PARAGRAPH, PERFORM...THRU etc. (was: First COBOL compiler)

_(reply depth: 12)_

- **From:** paulr@bix.com (paulr)
- **Date:** 1998-11-26T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<73iif7$k08@lotho.delphi.com>`
- **References:** `<eJ4nMCA$x$U2EwSx@hayford.demon.co.uk> <19981119102634.09602.00000036@ng-cr1.aol.com> <Tpc52.297$Sf1.435979@news3.mia> <3655866a.0@news3.uswest.net> <lSl52.587$Gy.710084@news4.mia> <3659bf9d.0@news2.uswest.net> <FNj62.2086$w_6.2122497@news2.mia> <73hs9r$gjj@lotho.delphi.com> <gG_62.5499$w_6.3386611@news2.mia>`

```
Probably a better option than perform through is to perform a single
paragraph and have that paragraph perform an subsequent paragraphs, 
returning  to the caller from the orignally called paragraph.

That sounds a lot more confusing than it really is, but it lends
a different mindset; the caller gives up execution control at 
the call, and the when of a return is determined by susequent 
processing. 

That is of course, how 99% of all function calls work, and procedure
calls in langs that support procedure calls. It allows you to better
encapsulate the processing logic, especially from a design standpoint
since you can defer implementation descisions to a much later point
in development.

Yes, I know that sounds bad to old school programmers, but it is actually
a very good way to design and implement. <grin> Come to think of it,
I just ran into that today and had to get rather stubborn.

-Paul

Agree completely. I read something many years ago when I followed motor
racing that the difference between the amateur and the professional is

copies in a row. I see the same observation applied to programming. I
also suspect that the bulk of us who are active on this NG really enjoy
programming, which probably outweighs any differences in concepts or
styles.

Bill Lynch
```

```
> I'll second that motion!  Consistency in whatever programming standards that
> a programmer adopts is the key to easier maintenance.  Really good
> programmers are so consistent in the work that others familiar with their
> style can recognize their work instantly when opening up a particular
> program for some maintenance work.

I knew a programmer who wanted to be a lawyer.  He was in love with
indices and tables, and liked flowery, hard to read documentation which
was as hard to understand as his code was.  We could easily recognize
his code and his comments.  And we all dreaded maintaining his code.

Consistency isn't everything!
```
